Modifying Particles: How Law, Lama, and Innama Distort Conditionals
Author discusses particles that 'distort' conditional propositions, adding extra implications or changing their form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the extra logical implication added by the particles law, lama, and innama to conditional propositions, and determine whether the proposition is closer to conditional or disjunctive form.

Core Idea
Particles like law, lama, and innama add extra implications beyond simple conditionality, distorting pure conditional or disjunctive form.

Conditional propositions often come with particles that add extra meaning. The author calls this 'tahrif' (distortion), not in a negative sense, but as an alteration of the pure logical form. Understanding these particles is crucial for correctly interpreting the logical force of statements in arguments, especially in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle in the conditional statement
Look for particles like 'لو' (law), 'لما' (lama), or 'إنما' (innama) that modify the conditional. Example: 'لو كانت الشمس طالعة لكان النهار موجوداً'.
2
Determine the extra implication of the particle
For 'law': it indicates that the antecedent is false because the consequent is false (contrary-to-fact). For 'lama': it indicates actual occurrence of both antecedent and consequent. For 'innama': it indicates that the consequent follows only from the antecedent (exclusivity).
The passage explains each: 'لو' تدل على عدم المقدم لعدم التالي; 'لما' تدل على الوقوع; 'إنما' تدل على تخصيص التالي باتباعه للمقدم.
3
Check if the proposition is pure or distorted
If the particle adds an implication not inherent in the pure conditional form (which only states a connection), then the proposition is 'محرفة' (distorted). For example, 'لو' implies the antecedent is false, which is not part of a pure conditional.
4
Decide if the proposition is closer to conditional or disjunctive
Some distorted propositions can be 'purified' by rewording. For example, 'لا تكون الشمس طالعة إلا والنهار موجود' can be recast as a conditional (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود) or as a disjunctive (إما أن لا تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً). The author says it is closer to a disjunctive because the change is less.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت التام والناقص من المنفصلات وما لفظة (إما) وإما فيها دالة على المنع من الجمع والخلو أو الجمع دون الخلو، أو الخلو دون الجمع، فاعلم أنه قد تترك أقضية من أجزاء تكون لفظة (إما ) وإما في بعضها دالة على المنع من الجمع والخلو، وفي بعضها دالة على المنع من الجمع دون الخلو، وفي بعضها دالة على المنع من الخلو دون الجمع، وقلما ينتفع بها أو تقع في العلوم كما في قولنا: (هذا الشيء إما حيوان وإما نبات وإما لا حيوان وإما لا (نبات فالنسبة بين الحيوان ولا حيوان والنبات ولا نبات نسبة مانعة من الجمع والخلو، والنسبة بين الحيوان والنبات مانعة من الجمع دون الخلو والنسبة بين لا نبات ولا حيوان مانعة من الخلو دون الجمع، وربما اقترن بالقضية الشرطية ألفاظ أخرى غير ما أشرنا إليها أوجبت تحريفاً إما بإثبات أحكام للقضية لا تقتضيها القضية لنفسها، وإما بإخراجها عن أن تكون متصلة محضة أو منفصلة محضة، وربما كانت ظاهرة في الاتصال وهي أقرب إلى الانفصال، وكذا بالعكس، أما الأول فمثل (لو ولما وإنما) فلفظة لو تدل مع الاشتراط على عدم المقدم لعدم التالي كما في قولك: (لو كانت الشمس طالعة لكان النهار موجوداً ولما أيضاً دالة مع الاتصال على الوقوع كما في قولك: (لو كانت السماء مصحية كانت الشمس (ظاهرة وإنما تدل على تخصيص التالي باتباعه للمقدم كما في قولك: (إنما النهار موجود إذا كانت الشمس طالعة، ولا يحسن استعمالها عند العكس.
جميع
وأما الثاني فكقولك: لا تكون الشمس طالعة إلا والنهار موجود) أو (حتى يكون النهار موجوداً) أو (يكون النهار موجوداً) فإن هذا القول، وإن كان شرطياً فليس بمتصل ولا منفصل محض، بل إن شئت محضته متصلاً بأن تحذف هذه الأدوات وتدخل أداة الاتصال فقلت إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار (موجود) وإن شئت محضته منفصلاً بأن تبق حرف السلب وتحذف ما سواه وتدخل أداة الانفصال فقلت إما أن لا تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً)، ولما كان التغيير في الانفصال أقل كان هذا القول بالمنفصلات أشبه، وعلى هذا قولك: قد تكون الشمس طالعة وليس الليل موجوداً فإنه وإن كان شرطياً وليس متمخضاً
And if you have understood the complete and incomplete disjunctives, and that the term 'imma' in them indicates either prevention of combination and void, or combination without void, or void without combination, then know that there may be composite propositions whose parts: in some of them the term 'imma' indicates prevention of combination and void, in some prevention of combination without void, and in some prevention of void without combination. They are rarely beneficial or occur in the sciences, as in our saying: (This thing is either an animal, or a plant, or not an animal, or not a plant). For the relationship between animal and not animal, and plant and not plant, is a relationship preventing combination and void; the relationship between animal and plant prevents combination but not void; and the relationship between not plant and not animal prevents void but not combination. And sometimes a conditional proposition is accompanied by other terms besides those we have indicated, which cause distortion (tahrif), either by establishing for the proposition rulings that the proposition itself does not require, or by removing it from being a pure conditional or a pure disjunctive. And sometimes it appears to be a conditional while it is closer to a disjunctive, and vice versa. As for the first, examples are 'law', 'lama', and 'innama'. The particle 'law' indicates, along with conditionality, the non-existence of the antecedent due to the non-existence of the consequent, as in your saying: (If the sun were rising, then the daytime would exist). And 'lama' also indicates, along with connection, actual occurrence, as in your saying: (If the sky were clear, the sun would be visible). And 'innama' indicates specification of the consequent by its following the antecedent, as in your saying: (The daytime exists only if the sun is rising), and it is not good to use it in the converse. As for the second, like your saying: (The sun is not rising unless daytime exists) or (until daytime exists) or (when daytime exists). For this statement, although conditional, is neither a pure conditional nor a pure disjunctive. Rather, if you wish, you can purify it as a conditional by deleting these particles and inserting the particle of connection, saying: (If the sun is rising, then daytime exists). And if you wish, you can purify it as a disjunctive by keeping the particle of negation and deleting other than it and inserting the particle of disjunction, saying: (Either the sun is not rising or daytime exists). And since the change in the disjunctive is less, this statement is more similar to disjunctives. Similarly, your saying: (The sun may be rising while nighttime does not exist) is also conditional and not pure.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage extends the classification of disjunctives to mixed forms and introduces particles that modify conditional propositions. The author first notes that some composite disjunctives have parts of different types: some parts prevent combination and void (complete), some prevent combination only, some prevent void only. An example is 'This thing is either an animal, or a plant, or not an animal, or not a plant.' The relationship between 'animal' and 'not animal' (and between 'plant' and 'not plant') is complete prevention; between 'animal' and 'plant' it prevents combination only (since something can be neither); between 'not plant' and 'not animal' it prevents void only (since something cannot be both not). The author remarks such propositions are rarely useful in the sciences. Then he turns to other particles that 'distort' (tahrif) conditional propositions, changing their logical character. Three types of distortion are mentioned: (1) particles that establish additional rulings not inherent in the proposition, or (2) remove it from being purely conditional or purely disjunctive, or (3) make it appear one way while being closer to the other. Specific particles: 'law' (if) indicates that the antecedent is false because the consequent is false; 'lama' (if) indicates actual occurrence with connection; 'innama' (only) specifies that the consequent follows the antecedent. For example, 'law' in 'If the sun were rising, daytime would exist' implies the sun is not rising because daytime does not exist. 'Lama' in 'If the sky were clear, the sun would be visible' implies the sky is clear and the sun is visible. 'Innama' in 'Daytime exists only if the sun is rising' cannot be used conversely. The second category includes phrases like 'The sun is not rising unless daytime exists,' which is not purely conditional or disjunctive. It can be purified as a conditional ('If the sun is rising, then daytime exists') or as a disjunctive ('Either the sun is not rising or daytime exists'). Since the change to a disjunctive involves fewer alterations, it is considered closer to a disjunctive. Similarly, 'The sun may be rising while nighttime does not exist' is conditional but not pure. This analysis is important for correctly interpreting the logical form of propositions in arguments.

Key Terms
تحريف
لو
لما
إنما
محضة

Checkpoint

A) لو (law)
B) لما (lama)
C) إنما (innama)
D) لا...إلا (la...illa)

Key Takeaway
Particles like law, lama, and innama add extra implications to conditional propositions, subtly altering their logical form; mastering these ensures accurate interpretation in argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'لولا الدواء لاشتد المرض' (If not for the medicine, the illness would have worsened). Identify the particle used and explain what extra implication it adds beyond simple conditionality. Then propose whether this statement is closer to a pure conditional or a pure disjunctive after purification.

Three Types of Prevention in Disjunctive Propositions with 'إما'
Author defines three types of prevention in disjunctive propositions with 'إما'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of prevention (man') in disjunctive propositions and apply them to examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Disjunction in Islamic logic has three distinct prevention types, not just exclusive OR.

In this passage, the author explains that when the particle 'إما' is repeated, it can indicate one of three logical relationships: prevention of both conjunction and emptiness (exclusive disjunction), prevention of conjunction only (not both true), or prevention of emptiness only (not both false). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences, especially when analyzing compound disjunctions in legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prevention types
List the three Arabic terms: 'المنع من الجمع والخلو' (prevention of both conjunction and emptiness), 'المنع من الجمع دون الخلو' (prevention of conjunction only), and 'المنع من الخلو دون الجمع' (prevention of emptiness only).
These correspond to exclusive OR, not both true, and not both false respectively.
2
Understand truth conditions for each type
For 'مانعة الجمع والخلو': exactly one alternative is true. For 'مانعة الجمع دون الخلو': at most one is true (both false allowed). For 'مانعة الخلو دون الجمع': at least one is true (both true allowed).
3
Analyze the author's animal-plant example
The example: 'هذا الشيء إما حيوان وإما نبات وإما لا حيوان وإما لا نبات'. Identify the relations: (حيوان/لا حيوان) and (نبات/لا نبات) are مانعة الجمع والخلو; (حيوان/نبات) is مانعة الجمع دون الخلو; (لا نبات/لا حيوان) is مانعة الخلو دون الجمع.
This shows that different pairs within a complex disjunction can have different prevention types.
4
Apply to a simple two-part disjunction
Construct a disjunction like 'إما أن يكون الفعل واجبا وإما أن يكون حراما'. Determine which prevention type applies if the act cannot be both but can be neither (مانعة الجمع دون الخلو), or cannot be neither but can be both (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت التام والناقص من المنفصلات وما لفظه (إما) وإما فيها دالة على المنع من الجمع والخلو أو الجمع دون الخلو، أو الخلو دون الجمع، فاعلم أنه قد تترك أقضية من أجزاء تكون لفظة (إما ) وإما في بعضها دالة على المنع من الجمع والخلو، وفي بعضها دالة على المنع من الجمع دون الخلو، وفي بعضها دالة على المنع من الخلو دون الجمع، وقلما ينتفع بها أو تقع في العلوم كما في قولنا: (هذا الشيء إما حيوان وإما نبات وإما لا حيوان وإما لا (نبات فالنسبة بين الحيوان ولا حيوان والنبات ولا نبات نسبة مانعة من الجمع والخلو، والنسبة بين الحيوان والنبات مانعة من الجمع دون الخلو والنسبة بين لا نبات ولا حيوان مانعة من الخلو دون الجمع.
And when you have known the complete and incomplete of disjunctives and what its wording is (إما) and إما in them indicates prevention of gathering and emptiness, or gathering without emptiness, or emptiness without gathering, then know that propositions may be left from parts where the word إما and إما in some of them indicates prevention of gathering and emptiness, and in some indicates prevention of gathering without emptiness, and in some indicates prevention of emptiness without gathering, and rarely are they beneficial or occur in the sciences, as in our statement: (This thing is either an animal or a plant or not an animal or not a plant, so the relation between animal and not animal and plant and not plant is a relation that prevents gathering and emptiness, and the relation between animal and plant prevents gathering without emptiness, and the relation between not plant and not animal prevents emptiness without gathering.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of prevention (man') in disjunctive propositions containing the particle 'إما'. The author first reminds the reader of the distinction between complete and incomplete disjunctives. He then explains that when the particle 'إما' is repeated, it can indicate one of three meanings: prevention of both gathering and emptiness (مانعة الجمع والخلو), prevention of gathering only (مانعة الجمع دون الخلو), and prevention of emptiness only (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع). He notes that such compound disjunctives are rarely used in the sciences. He provides an example involving a four-part disjunction: 'This thing is either an animal or a plant or not an animal or not a plant.' He analyzes the relations: the pairs (animal/not animal) and (plant/not plant) are mutually exclusive and exhaustive (مانعة الجمع والخلو), while (animal/plant) are only exclusive of gathering (مانعة الجمع دون الخلو), and (not plant/not animal) are only exclusive of emptiness (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع). This illustrates how different pairs within a single complex disjunction can have different logical properties.

إما
المنع من الجمع والخلو
المنع من الجمع دون الخلو
المنع من الخلو دون الجمع

Checkpoint
What does 'مانعة الجمع دون الخلو' mean in a disjunctive proposition?
A) It prevents both alternatives from being true together.
B) It prevents both alternatives from being false together.
C) It prevents only the conjunction (both true) but allows both false.
D) It prevents only the emptiness (both false) but allows both true.

Key Takeaway
The three prevention types in disjunctive propositions are defined by whether they prevent conjunction (both true), emptiness (both false), or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following disjunction: 'هذا الفعل إما واجب وإما حرام وإما مباح' – determine which prevention type applies to each pair (واجب/حرام, واجب/مباح, حرام/مباح) according to the logic of the passage.
?
Find a real disjunction from a fiqh or kalam text (e.g., 'العالم إما قديم وإما حادث') and identify which prevention type it most likely uses, and justify.

Propositions

Distorting Particles in Conditional Propositions
Author explains the distorting effects of لو, لما, and إنما on conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical implications of the particles لو, لما, and إنما in conditional statements from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
These particles add extra meanings that hide the pure conditional form.

In the previous lesson, we studied pure conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals. However, real Arabic conditionals often include particles like لو, لما, and إنما that add extra implications of falsity, actuality, or exclusivity. Understanding these particles is essential for correctly interpreting conditional statements in the Qur'an, Hadith, and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the particle in the conditional
When reading a conditional statement, look for لو, لما, or إنما. Note that these are not the standard conditionals (إن, إذا).
2
Determine the added implication
For لو, it implies that both antecedent and consequent are false. For لما, it implies actual occurrence of the consequent when the antecedent occurs. For إنما, it restricts the consequent exclusively to the antecedent.
3
Test with an example from Islamic sciences
Consider a statement like 'لو كان الله يريد الترفيه لخلق لعبا' (If God intended amusement, He would have created playthings). This implies God does not intend amusement and did not create playthings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما اقترن بالقضية الشرطية ألفاظ أخرى غير ما أشرنا إليها أوجبت تحريفاً إما بإثبات أحكام للقضية لا تقتضيها القضية لنفسها، وإما بإخراجها عن أن تكون متصلة محضة أو منفصلة محضة، وربما كانت ظاهرة في الاتصال وهي أقرب إلى الانفصال، وكذا بالعكس، أما الأول فمثل (لو ولما وإنما) فلفظة لو تدل مع الاشتراط على عدم المقدم لعدم التالي كما في قولك: (لو كانت الشمس طالعة لكان النهار موجوداً ولما أيضاً دالة مع الاتصال على الوقوع كما في قولك: (لو كانت السماء مصحية كانت الشمس (ظاهرة وإنما تدل على تخصيص التالي باتباعه للمقدم كما في قولك: (إنما النهار موجود إذا كانت الشمس طالعة، ولا يحسن استعمالها عند العكس. وأما الثاني فكقولك: لا تكون الشمس طالعة إلا والنهار موجود) أو (حتى يكون النهار موجوداً) أو (يكون النهار موجوداً) فإن هذا القول، وإن كان شرطياً فليس بمتصل ولا منفصل محض، بل إن شئت محضته متصلاً بأن تحذف هذه الأدوات وتدخل أداة الاتصال فقلت إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار (موجود) وإن شئت محضته منفصلاً بأن تبقي حرف السلب وتحذف ما سواه وتدخل أداة الانفصال فقلت إما أن لا تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً)، ولما كان التغيير في الانفصال أقل كان هذا القول بالمنفصلات أشبه، وعلى هذا قولك: قد تكون الشمس طالعة وليس الليل موجوداً فإنه وإن كان شرطياً وليس متمخضاً للاتصال ولا للانفصال فأقرب إلى الاتصال، إذ لا يفتقر مع إدخال أداة الاتصال إلى تغيير هذه الأدوات، فإنك تقول قد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل ليس بموجود) ولا تقدر على ذلك في الانفصال إلا بحذف هذه الأدوات أو بعضها، وهو أن تقول (إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً).
And perhaps other words besides what we indicated may accompany the conditional proposition, causing distortion, either by affirming rulings for the proposition that the proposition itself does not entail, or by removing it from being a pure conjunctive or a pure disjunctive. And perhaps it may appear to be conjunctive while being closer to disjunctive, and vice versa. As for the first, examples are (لو and لما and إنما). So the word 'لو' indicates, along with conditionality, the non-existence of the antecedent due to the non-existence of the consequent, as in your saying: 'If the sun were shining, the daytime would exist.' And 'لما' also indicates, along with conjunction, occurrence, as in your saying: 'If the sky were clear, the sun would be visible.' And 'إنما' indicates specification of the consequent by its following the antecedent, as in your saying: 'The daytime exists only if the sun is shining.' And it is not appropriate to use it in the converse. As for the second, it is like your saying: 'The sun is not shining unless the daytime exists' or 'until the daytime exists' or 'the daytime exists'. For this statement, although conditional, is neither pure conjunctive nor pure disjunctive. Rather, if you wish, you can make it pure conjunctive by deleting these particles and inserting the particle of conjunction, saying: 'If the sun is shining, then the daytime exists.' And if you wish, you can make it pure disjunctive by retaining the particle of negation and deleting what else and inserting the particle of disjunction, saying: 'Either the sun is not shining or the daytime exists.' And since the change in the disjunctive is less, this statement is more similar to disjunctives. And likewise your saying: 'It may be that the sun is shining and the night does not exist.' For it, although conditional, is not purely conjunctive or disjunctive, but is closer to conjunctive, since with the introduction of the particle of conjunction it does not require changing these particles. So you say: 'It may be that if the sun is shining, then the night does not exist.' And you cannot do that in the disjunctive except by deleting these particles or some of them, which is to say: 'Either the sun is shining or the night exists.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of conditional propositions by examining other particles that can accompany them and alter their logical character. The author identifies two main distortions: (1) some particles add extra meaning not inherent in the conditional itself, (2) some conditionals are ambiguous between conjunctive and disjunctive forms. He gives examples of the first type: 'لو' (if...then but with implication of non-occurrence), 'لما' (when; indicates actual occurrence), and 'إنما' (only; specifies that the consequent follows only from that antecedent). For the second type, he discusses statements like 'The sun is not shining unless the daytime exists' which can be transformed into either a pure conjunctive (if...then) or pure disjunctive (either...or) with minimal changes. He notes that such statements are closer to disjunctive because less change is required. Another example is 'It may be that the sun is shining and the night does not exist' which is closer to conjunctive because it can be made conjunctive without deleting particles. This illustrates that real usage of conditional propositions often deviates from the pure logical forms, and logicians must analyze them carefully.

Key Terms
لو
لما
إنما

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which particle indicates that both the antecedent and the consequent are false?
A) إنما
B) لو
C) لما
D) إذا

Key Takeaway
The particles لو, لما, and إنما each add a specific extra meaning to a conditional statement that changes its logical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the particle in the following conditional from a theological argument: 'لما جاء محمد بالقرآن آمن به كثيرون' (When Muhammad brought the Quran, many believed). Determine what implication it carries. Convert the statement into a pure conjunctive conditional.

Purifying Mixed Conditionals
Author demonstrates how to purify mixed conditionals into pure conjunctive or disjunctive forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to transform a mixed conditional statement into either a pure conjunctive or pure disjunctive form by applying minimal changes.

Core Idea
Mixed conditionals can be purified to their logical core with a few systematic adjustments.

Not every conditional in Arabic is a pure if-then or either-or. Many statements like 'The sun is not shining unless the daytime exists' or 'It may be that the sun is shining and the night does not exist' are mixed. The logician must be able to 'purify' them to evaluate their logical form. This lesson shows how to transform such statements into pure conjunctive or disjunctive conditionals with minimal changes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed conditional
Look for particles like 'إلا', 'حتى', 'قد' that indicate the statement is not a pure conditional. Also note the presence of negation or possibility indicators.
2
Determine whether it is closer to conjunctive or disjunctive
The author gives a rule: if the statement can be made conjunctive without deleting particles, it is closer to conjunctive. If it requires less change to become disjunctive, it is closer to disjunctive.
3
Apply the transformation to pure conjunctive
Delete particles that are not part of the pure conditional and add a standard conditional connector like 'إن' or 'إذا'. For example, 'لا تكون الشمس طالعة إلا والنهار موجود' becomes 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود'.
4
Apply the transformation to pure disjunctive
Retain negation and use 'إما...إما'. The example becomes 'إما أن لا تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجودا'.
5
Check which transformation requires fewer changes
The author says the disjunctive transformation for that example has less change, so it is more similar to disjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما اقترن بالقضية الشرطية ألفاظ أخرى غير ما أشرنا إليها أوجبت تحريفاً إما بإثبات أحكام للقضية لا تقتضيها القضية لنفسها، وإما بإخراجها عن أن تكون متصلة محضة أو منفصلة محضة، وربما كانت ظاهرة في الاتصال وهي أقرب إلى الانفصال، وكذا بالعكس، أما الأول فمثل (لو ولما وإنما) فلفظة لو تدل مع الاشتراط على عدم المقدم لعدم التالي كما في قولك: (لو كانت الشمس طالعة لكان النهار موجوداً ولما أيضاً دالة مع الاتصال على الوقوع كما في قولك: (لو كانت السماء مصحية كانت الشمس (ظاهرة وإنما تدل على تخصيص التالي باتباعه للمقدم كما في قولك: (إنما النهار موجود إذا كانت الشمس طالعة، ولا يحسن استعمالها عند العكس. وأما الثاني فكقولك: لا تكون الشمس طالعة إلا والنهار موجود) أو (حتى يكون النهار موجوداً) أو (يكون النهار موجوداً) فإن هذا القول، وإن كان شرطياً فليس بمتصل ولا منفصل محض، بل إن شئت محضته متصلاً بأن تحذف هذه الأدوات وتدخل أداة الاتصال فقلت إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار (موجود) وإن شئت محضته منفصلاً بأن تبقي حرف السلب وتحذف ما سواه وتدخل أداة الانفصال فقلت إما أن لا تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً)، ولما كان التغيير في الانفصال أقل كان هذا القول بالمنفصلات أشبه، وعلى هذا قولك: قد تكون الشمس طالعة وليس الليل موجوداً فإنه وإن كان شرطياً وليس متمخضاً للاتصال ولا للانفصال فأقرب إلى الاتصال، إذ لا يفتقر مع إدخال أداة الاتصال إلى تغيير هذه الأدوات، فإنك تقول قد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل ليس بموجود) ولا تقدر على ذلك في الانفصال إلا بحذف هذه الأدوات أو بعضها، وهو أن تقول (إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً).
And perhaps other words besides what we indicated may accompany the conditional proposition, causing distortion, either by affirming rulings for the proposition that the proposition itself does not entail, or by removing it from being a pure conjunctive or a pure disjunctive. And perhaps it may appear to be conjunctive while being closer to disjunctive, and vice versa. As for the first, examples are (لو and لما and إنما). So the word 'لو' indicates, along with conditionality, the non-existence of the antecedent due to the non-existence of the consequent, as in your saying: 'If the sun were shining, the daytime would exist.' And 'لما' also indicates, along with conjunction, occurrence, as in your saying: 'If the sky were clear, the sun would be visible.' And 'إنما' indicates specification of the consequent by its following the antecedent, as in your saying: 'The daytime exists only if the sun is shining.' And it is not appropriate to use it in the converse. As for the second, it is like your saying: 'The sun is not shining unless the daytime exists' or 'until the daytime exists' or 'the daytime exists'. For this statement, although conditional, is neither pure conjunctive nor pure disjunctive. Rather, if you wish, you can make it pure conjunctive by deleting these particles and inserting the particle of conjunction, saying: 'If the sun is shining, then the daytime exists.' And if you wish, you can make it pure disjunctive by retaining the particle of negation and deleting what else and inserting the particle of disjunction, saying: 'Either the sun is not shining or the daytime exists.' And since the change in the disjunctive is less, this statement is more similar to disjunctives. And likewise your saying: 'It may be that the sun is shining and the night does not exist.' For it, although conditional, is not purely conjunctive or disjunctive, but is closer to conjunctive, since with the introduction of the particle of conjunction it does not require changing these particles. So you say: 'It may be that if the sun is shining, then the night does not exist.' And you cannot do that in the disjunctive except by deleting these particles or some of them, which is to say: 'Either the sun is shining or the night exists.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of conditional propositions by examining other particles that can accompany them and alter their logical character. The author identifies two main distortions: (1) some particles add extra meaning not inherent in the conditional itself, (2) some conditionals are ambiguous between conjunctive and disjunctive forms. He gives examples of the first type: 'لو' (if...then but with implication of non-occurrence), 'لما' (when; indicates actual occurrence), and 'إنما' (only; specifies that the consequent follows only from that antecedent). For the second type, he discusses statements like 'The sun is not shining unless the daytime exists' which can be transformed into either a pure conjunctive (if...then) or pure disjunctive (either...or) with minimal changes. He notes that such statements are closer to disjunctive because less change is required. Another example is 'It may be that the sun is shining and the night does not exist' which is closer to conjunctive because it can be made conjunctive without deleting particles. This illustrates that real usage of conditional propositions often deviates from the pure logical forms, and logicians must analyze them carefully.

Key Terms
متصلة محضة
منفصلة محضة

Mixed conditionals can be analyzed by transforming them into pure conjunctive or disjunctive forms, and the transformation requiring fewer changes indicates the statement's natural logical affinity.

Why the Conditional is Closer to Connection
Author explains why the conditional is closer to connection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disjoined conditional propositions by analyzing the linguistic tools used in each.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions naturally align with connection, not disjunction.

In this passage, the author argues that conditional propositions (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) are fundamentally closer to connection (ittisaal) than disconnection (infisaal). The proof lies in how easily the tools of connection (like 'if...then') integrate into the statement without changing its components, whereas expressing the same meaning as a disjunction forces you to delete or modify those tools. Let's examine this distinction step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of a conditional
A conditional proposition has two components: the fore (muqaddam) and the aft (tali). In the example, 'the sun is rising' is the fore and 'the night is not existent' is the aft.
2
Notice the tools of connection
The connected conditional uses particles like 'in' (if) and 'fa' (then). These are added directly without altering the wording of the fore and aft.
3
Notice the tools of disjunction
The disjoined conditional uses 'imma...imma' (either...or). To express the same meaning as a disjunction, you must drop the 'if' and 'then' and restructure the sentence.
4
Compare the two expressions
In the connected form: 'If the sun is rising, then the night is not existent.' The components remain unchanged. In the disjoined form: 'Either the sun is rising or the night is existent.' The aft is transformed from negative to positive, showing that the tool of disjunction forces change.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since connection requires no alteration of the components while disjunction does, the conditional proposition is inherently closer to connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني: في القضايا الشرطية للاتصال ولا للانفصال فأقرب إلى الاتصال، إذ لا يفتقر مع إدخال أداة الاتصال إلى تغيير هذه الأدوات، فإنك تقول (قد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل ليس بموجود) ولا تقدر على ذلك في الانفصال إلا بحذف هذه الأدوات أو بعضها، وهو أن تقول (إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً).
The second art: On conditional propositions. [It is] closer to connection than to disconnection, because when introducing the tool of connection, it does not require changing these tools. For you say: (It may be that if the sun is rising, then the night is not existent). And you are not able to do that in disconnection except by deleting these tools or some of them, and that is to say: (Either the sun is rising or the night is existent).
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage defines a property of conditional propositions: they are more akin to connected (متصلة) than to disjoined (منفصلة) conditionals. The author argues that the tools used to express connection (such as إن and الفاء) can be added without altering the original propositional components, whereas for disjunction (إما...إما) one must delete some of these tools. For example, the connected conditional 'If the sun is rising, then the night is not existent' uses the particle 'if' and 'then' without changing the components. To express the same meaning as a disjunction, one must drop those particles and say 'Either the sun is rising or the night is existent', which changes the structure. Thus, the conditional proposition is inherently closer to connection.

Key Terms
القضية الشرطية
الاتصال
الانفصال

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are inherently linked to connection because connection tools integrate seamlessly without altering the proposition's components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Basic Composition of Conditionals
Author defines the composition of conditional propositions and lists their possible combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two categorical propositions that form the foundation of any conditional proposition.

Core Idea
Every conditional resolves into two categoricals upon analysis.

This lesson presents a foundational principle in logic: conditional propositions are not irreducible; rather, they are composed of simpler categoricals. The author of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq makes explicit that every conditional, whether connected or disjoined, analytically must terminate in categoricals. Understanding this composition is essential for deconstructing complex arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional type
Determine whether the proposition is a connected conditional (using 'if...then') or a disjoined conditional (using 'either...or'). This distinction affects how you extract the parts.
The passage distinguishes between متصلة and منفصلة.
2
Extract the two parts
For a connected conditional, isolate the antecedent (مقدم) and the consequent (تالي). For a disjoined conditional, isolate the two disjuncts (جزئان). These are the component propositions.
Each part is a complete proposition before the conditional particle is added.
3
Remove conditional particles
Strip away particles such as 'إن', 'فاء', 'إما' to see the naked categorical propositions. For example, remove 'إن' from 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة' to get 'الشمس طالعة'.
The passage notes that these particles 'remove the proposition from being categorical'.
4
Verify each part is categorical
Check that each resulting proposition has a subject and predicate without any conditional or disjunctive operator. If not, it requires further analysis.
The passage emphasizes that at minimum, two categoricals are needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول: إن القضية الشرطية سواء كانت متصلة أو منفصلة، قد ثبت أن النسبة الخبرية لكل واحد من جزأيها عند التحليل لا بدّ فيها من الانتهاء إلى الحمليات، فلا جرم كان أقل ما تأتلف الشرطية من حملتين إما في المتصلة، فكقولنا: (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود) وفي المنفصلة كقولنا: إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً فكل واحدة منهما مؤلفة من قضيتين حملتين اقترن بكل واحدة منهما ما أخرجها عن الخبرية لو حذف عنها كانت خبراً، وذلك مثل (إن) في قولك: الشمس طالعة) و (الفاء) في قولك: النهار موجود وأما في قولك: العدد زوج العدد فرد وقد تزيد على ذلك بأن تكون من متصلتين أو منفصلتين أو حملية ومتصلة أو حملية ومنفصلة أو متصلة ومنفصلة، ولنوضح مثال ذلك في المتصلات ونتلوه بالمنفصلات، فإن كانت المتصلة من متصلتين كقولك: إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فكل ما كان الليل موجوداً فالشمس غاربة وإن كانت من منفصلتين فكقولك: إن كان إما أن يكون الجوهر موجوداً في موضوع وإما موجوداً لا في موضوع فبعض الموجودات إما موجود في موضوع وإما موجود لا في موضوع، وإن كانت من حملية ومتصلة، فأما أن تكون الحملية مقدماً أو تالياً، فإن كانت مقدماً فكما في قولنا: (إن كانت الشمس علة وجود النهار فكل ما كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة) وإن كانت الحملية تالياً فكقولنا: (إن كان كل ما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فالشمس على النهار) وإن كانت حملية ومنفصلة فأما أن تكون الحملية أيضاً مقدماً أو تالياً، فإن كانت مقدماً فكقولنا: (إن كانت الشمس على النهار فإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً وإما أن تكون الشمس طالعة) وإن كانت تالياً فكقولنا: (إن كان إما أن يكون الليل موجوداً وإما أن تكون الشمس طالعة فالشمس)
We say: The conditional proposition, whether connected or disjoined, it has been established that the predicative relation of each of its two parts, upon analysis, must end in categoricals. Thus, the minimum composition of a conditional is from two categoricals. In the connected, it is like our saying: (If the sun is rising, then the day exists). In the disjoined, like our saying: Either the number is even or the number is odd. Each of these is composed of two categorical propositions, each accompanied by something that removes it from being categorical; if that were removed, it would be a categorical. That is like (إن) in your saying: the sun is rising, and (الفاء) in your saying: the day exists. As for your saying: the number is even, the number is odd. And it may exceed that by being composed of two connecteds, or two disjoineds, or a categorical and a connected, or a categorical and a disjoined, or a connected and a disjoined. Let us clarify an example of that in the connecteds and follow it with the disjoineds. If the connected is from two connecteds, like your saying: If whenever the sun is rising the day exists, then whenever the night exists the sun is setting. If it is from two disjoineds, like your saying: If either the substance exists in a subject or exists not in a subject, then some existents are either existent in a subject or existent not in a subject. If it is from a categorical and a connected, then either the categorical is the antecedent or the consequent. If it is the antecedent, as in our saying: (If the sun is the cause of the existence of day, then whenever the day exists the sun is rising). If the categorical is the consequent, as in our saying: (If whenever the sun is rising the day exists, then the sun is over the day). If it is from a categorical and a disjoined, then either the categorical is also the antecedent or the consequent. If it is the antecedent, as in our saying: (If the sun is over the day, then either the night exists or the sun is rising). If it is the consequent, as in our saying: (If either the night exists or the sun is rising, then the sun...)
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines the composition of conditional propositions. The author begins by stating that any conditional, whether connected or disjoined, ultimately resolves into categorical propositions upon analysis. Consequently, the simplest conditional is composed of two categoricals. For connected: 'If the sun is rising, then the day exists' contains two categoricals: 'the sun is rising' and 'the day exists'. For disjoined: 'Either the number is even or the number is odd' contains two categoricals. Each of these categoricals has a particle that removes it from being declarative (خبرية). Then the author explains that conditionals can be more complex: they can be composed of two connecteds, two disjoineds, or a mixture of a categorical with a connected or disjoined, and also a connected with a disjoined. Several examples are given using familiar physical concepts (sun, day, night) and logical concepts (substance, subject). The passage is enumerative, so we can list the types: (1) two connecteds, (2) two disjoineds, (3) categorical with connected (antecedent or consequent), (4) categorical with disjoined (antecedent or consequent), (5) connected with disjoined (not exemplified but implied).

Key Terms
القضية الشرطية
الحملية
المتصلة
المنفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One

C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Every conditional proposition is analytically reducible to at least two categorical propositions.

Complex Composition Types of Conditionals
Author defines the composition of conditional propositions and lists their possible combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional proposition into one of six complex composition types based on the nature of its parts.

Core Idea
Conditionals can be nested, forming hierarchies of logical structure.

While the simplest conditional consists of two categoricals, the author of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq explains that conditionals can be far more intricate. Their component parts can themselves be conditionals—connected or disjoined—or a mix of categorical and conditional. This lesson systematically explores these possibilities, using examples from the text to build your ability to parse complex logical statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conditional type
Determine if the main conditional is connected (متصلة) or disjoined (منفصلة). This sets the context for what follows.
The passage gives examples for both.
2
Analyze each component part
For the antecedent and consequent (or two disjuncts), classify each as: a categorical (حملية), a connected conditional (متصلة), or a disjoined conditional (منفصلة).
Use the particles as clues: 'إن...ف' for connected, 'إما...أو' for disjoined.
3
Match to a composition type
Compare your classification to the types listed in the passage: (1) two connecteds, (2) two disjoineds, (3) categorical antecedent + connected consequent, (4) connected antecedent + categorical consequent, (5) categorical antecedent + disjoined consequent, (6) disjoined antecedent + categorical consequent, (7) connected + disjoined (order matters).
The passage elaborates subcases for categorical with connected/disjoined.
4
Verify with a concrete example
Take one of the author's examples and confirm that the identified structure matches your analysis. For instance, 'If whenever the sun rises the day exists, then whenever the night exists the sun is setting' has a connected antecedent and a connected consequent.
This step ensures you can apply the classification in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول: إن القضية الشرطية سواء كانت متصلة أو منفصلة، قد ثبت أن النسبة الخبرية لكل واحد من جزأيها عند التحليل لا بدّ فيها من الانتهاء إلى الحمليات، فلا جرم كان أقل ما تأتلف الشرطية من حملتين إما في المتصلة، فكقولنا: (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود) وفي المنفصلة كقولنا: إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً فكل واحدة منهما مؤلفة من قضيتين حملتين اقترن بكل واحدة منهما ما أخرجها عن الخبرية لو حذف عنها كانت خبراً، وذلك مثل (إن) في قولك: الشمس طالعة) و (الفاء) في قولك: النهار موجود وأما في قولك: العدد زوج العدد فرد وقد تزيد على ذلك بأن تكون من متصلتين أو منفصلتين أو حملية ومتصلة أو حملية ومنفصلة أو متصلة ومنفصلة، ولنوضح مثال ذلك في المتصلات ونتلوه بالمنفصلات، فإن كانت المتصلة من متصلتين كقولك: إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فكل ما كان الليل موجوداً فالشمس غاربة وإن كانت من منفصلتين فكقولك: إن كان إما أن يكون الجوهر موجوداً في موضوع وإما موجوداً لا في موضوع فبعض الموجودات إما موجود في موضوع وإما موجود لا في موضوع، وإن كانت من حملية ومتصلة، فأما أن تكون الحملية مقدماً أو تالياً، فإن كانت مقدماً فكما في قولنا: (إن كانت الشمس علة وجود النهار فكل ما كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة) وإن كانت الحملية تالياً فكقولنا: (إن كان كل ما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فالشمس على النهار) وإن كانت حملية ومنفصلة فأما أن تكون الحملية أيضاً مقدماً أو تالياً، فإن كانت مقدماً فكقولنا: (إن كانت الشمس على النهار فإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً وإما أن تكون الشمس طالعة) وإن كانت تالياً فكقولنا: (إن كان إما أن يكون الليل موجوداً وإما أن تكون الشمس طالعة فالشمس)
We say: The conditional proposition, whether connected or disjoined, it has been established that the predicative relation of each of its two parts, upon analysis, must end in categoricals. Thus, the minimum composition of a conditional is from two categoricals. In the connected, it is like our saying: (If the sun is rising, then the day exists). In the disjoined, like our saying: Either the number is even or the number is odd. Each of these is composed of two categorical propositions, each accompanied by something that removes it from being categorical; if that were removed, it would be a categorical. That is like (إن) in your saying: the sun is rising, and (الفاء) in your saying: the day exists. As for your saying: the number is even, the number is odd. And it may exceed that by being composed of two connecteds, or two disjoineds, or a categorical and a connected, or a categorical and a disjoined, or a connected and a disjoined. Let us clarify an example of that in the connecteds and follow it with the disjoineds. If the connected is from two connecteds, like your saying: If whenever the sun is rising the day exists, then whenever the night exists the sun is setting. If it is from two disjoineds, like your saying: If either the substance exists in a subject or exists not in a subject, then some existents are either existent in a subject or existent not in a subject. If it is from a categorical and a connected, then either the categorical is the antecedent or the consequent. If it is the antecedent, as in our saying: (If the sun is the cause of the existence of day, then whenever the day exists the sun is rising). If the categorical is the consequent, as in our saying: (If whenever the sun is rising the day exists, then the sun is over the day). If it is from a categorical and a disjoined, then either the categorical is also the antecedent or the consequent. If it is the antecedent, as in our saying: (If the sun is over the day, then either the night exists or the sun is rising). If it is the consequent, as in our saying: (If either the night exists or the sun is rising, then the sun...)
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines the composition of conditional propositions. The author begins by stating that any conditional, whether connected or disjoined, ultimately resolves into categorical propositions upon analysis. Consequently, the simplest conditional is composed of two categoricals. For connected: 'If the sun is rising, then the day exists' contains two categoricals: 'the sun is rising' and 'the day exists'. For disjoined: 'Either the number is even or the number is odd' contains two categoricals. Each of these categoricals has a particle that removes it from being declarative (خبرية). Then the author explains that conditionals can be more complex: they can be composed of two connecteds, two disjoineds, or a mixture of a categorical with a connected or disjoined, and also a connected with a disjoined. Several examples are given using familiar physical concepts (sun, day, night) and logical concepts (substance, subject). The passage is enumerative, so we can list the types: (1) two connecteds, (2) two disjoineds, (3) categorical with connected (antecedent or consequent), (4) categorical with disjoined (antecedent or consequent), (5) connected with disjoined (not exemplified but implied).

The Composition of Conditional Propositions
Definition of conditional proposition composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two categorical components and connectors in any conjunctive or disjunctive conditional proposition from Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
Every conditional proposition reduces to two categoricals connected by a conditional operator.

This passage establishes the foundational structure of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya). Understanding that every conditional, whether 'if...then' or 'either...or', is composed of two categorical statements is essential for analysing complex arguments in Islamic logic, especially in legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional type
Determine whether the proposition is conjunctive (muttasila) using 'if...then' or disjunctive (munfasila) using 'either...or'.
This classification affects how the proposition is analysed.
2
Locate the two component parts
Separate the conditional into its two main clauses. For example, in 'If the sun is shining, then the daytime exists', the parts are 'the sun is shining' and 'the daytime exists'.
Each part should be a complete potential assertion.
3
Remove the conditional connectors
Delete words like 'if', 'then', 'either', 'or' to reveal the underlying categorical propositions. What remains should be a simple subject-predicate statement.
This shows that conditionals are built from categoricals.
4
Verify the reduction to categoricals
Check that each stripped part is a genuine categorical proposition (hamliyya) with a subject and predicate. If so, the analysis is complete.
The author asserts this reduction is necessary for all conditionals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في تعريف تأليف القضايا الشرطية وما تأتلف منه وكيفية المنحرفة منها وما به اتفاقها وافتراقها نقول: إن القضية الشرطية [سواء] كانت متصلة أو منفصلة، قد ثبت أن النسبة الخبرية لكل واحد من جزأيها عند التحليل لا بدّ فيها من الانتهاء إلى الحَمْليات، فلا جرم كان أقل ما تأتلف الشرطية من حَمْليتين إما في المتصلة، فكقولنا: (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار (موجود) وفي المنفصلة كقولنا: إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً فكل واحدة منهما مؤلفة من قضيتين حَمْليتين اقترن بكل واحدة منهما ما أخرجها عن الخبرية لو حذف عنها كانت خبراً، وذلك مثل (إن) في قولك: الشمس طالعة) و (الفاء) في قولك: النهار) (موجود) وأما في قولك: العدد زوج العدد (فرد)
Chapter Two: On the definition of the composition of conditional propositions, what they are composed of, the manner of their deviating forms, and what pertains to their agreement and difference. We say: The conditional proposition, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, has been established that the predicative relation of each of its two parts, upon analysis, must necessarily terminate in categoricals. Therefore, the least amount from which the conditional is composed is two categoricals. For the conjunctive, as in our saying: 'If the sun is shining, then the daytime exists.' And for the disjunctive, as in our saying: 'Either the number is even or it is odd.' Each one of them is composed of two categorical propositions, each of which is accompanied by something that removes its predicative nature; if that were removed, it would be a predication. That is like 'in' in your saying: 'the sun is shining', and 'fa' in your saying: 'the daytime exists'. And as for your saying: 'the number is even, the number (odd)'.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines the composition of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya). The author states that every conditional, whether conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila), must ultimately reduce to categorical propositions (hamliyya) upon analysis. The minimal composition is from two categoricals. For the conjunctive, the example is 'If the sun is shining, then the daytime exists' – here 'If' (in) and 'then' (fa) are connectors that remove the independent assertive force of each part. For the disjunctive, the example is 'Either the number is even or it is odd' – here the connector 'either/or' (imma...aw) serves the same purpose. The author emphasizes that each part, if stripped of its connector, would be a simple categorical statement. This is foundational for understanding more complex compositions later.

Types of Complex Conditional Composition
Enumerates all possible compositions of complex conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any complex conditional composition by identifying its component proposition types and the order of antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A conditional can be composed from any two proposition types in any order.

The text systematically enumerates every possible combination of conjunctive, disjunctive, and categorical propositions that form a complex conditional. This parsing exercise trains you to recognize the structure of nested conditionals, a skill needed to evaluate compound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall type
Determine if the complex proposition is a conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila) based on the main connective. The author first discusses conjunctive, then disjunctive.
2
List the component types
For a conjunctive complex proposition, the two components can be: both conjunctives, both disjunctives, a categorical and a conjunctive, a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conjunctive and a disjunctive.
The same possibilities exist for a disjunctive complex proposition.
3
Determine the order
When the components are of different types, check whether the first type is the antecedent (muqaddam) or consequent (tali). The author provides examples for both orders (e.g., categorical as antecedent or consequent).
In muttasila, the order matters; in munfasila, the parts are symmetric.
4
Verify the example
Test the given example against logical meaning. For instance, the conjunctive-from-two-conjunctives case: 'If whenever the sun is shining then daytime exists, then whenever night exists the sun is setting.' This nested conditional is logically valid.
Disjunctive examples may use repeated 'إما إما' for emphasis; focus on the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it may increase beyond that, by being from two conjunctives, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conjunctive, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conjunctive and a disjunctive. Let us clarify an example of that for conjunctives, and follow it with disjunctives. If the conjunctive is from two conjunctives, as in your saying: 'If whenever the sun is shining then daytime exists, then whenever night exists then the sun is setting.' And if from two disjunctives, as in your saying: 'If either the substance exists in a subject or exists not in a subject, then some existents either exist in a subject or do not exist in a subject.' And if from a categorical and a conjunctive, then either the categorical is the antecedent or the consequent. And if the categorical is consequent, as in our saying: 'If whenever the sun is shining then daytime exists, then the sun is the cause of daytime.' And if from a categorical and a disjunctive, then the categorical is either antecedent or consequent. If it is antecedent, as in our saying: 'If the sun is the cause of daytime, then either night exists or the sun is shining.' And if it is consequent, as in our saying: 'If either night exists or the sun is shining, then the sun is the cause of daytime.' And if it is consequent, as in our saying: 'If either the sun is shining or night exists, then whenever the sun is shining then daytime exists.' And if from a categorical and a conjunctive, as in our saying: 'Either the sun is the cause of daytime, or whenever the sun is shining then daytime does not exist.' Commentary
This passage systematically enumerates all possible complex compositions of conditional propositions. The author categorizes the combinations: a conjunctive can be composed from two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical and conjunctive (with the categorical as antecedent or consequent), a categorical and disjunctive (again with two orders), and a conjunctive and disjunctive (again with two orders). Similarly, a disjunctive can be composed from two disjunctives, two conjunctives, a categorical and disjunctive, a categorical and conjunctive, and a conjunctive and disjunctive. Each combination is illustrated with a logical example drawn from natural phenomena and everyday logic. For instance, a conjunctive from two conjunctives: 'If whenever the sun is shining then daytime exists, then whenever night exists the sun is setting.' This shows the nesting of conditionals. The examples serve as a parsing exercise: the student must identify the type of composition, the roles of antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali), and ensure the logical structure is sound. The disjunctive examples often involve alternatives, such as 'Either this existent is a substance or an accident, or it is not a substance and not an accident' – a more complex disjunction. Notably, the author uses the phrase 'إما إما' which may indicate a repeated 'either' for emphasis, but the structure remains clear. This enumeration is essential for understanding the full flexibility of conditional logic in the Islamic tradition.

Distinctive Properties of Disjunctive and Conjunctive Propositions
Explains indifference of order in disjunctives and limitation of conjunctives to two parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish disjunctive from conjunctive propositions based on order indifference and allowable number of parts, and apply these distinctions to Islamic logical examples.

Core Idea
Disjunctives have no fixed order and can have many parts; conjunctives are strictly binary and ordered.

In this lesson, we explore two key contrasts between disjunctive and conjunctive propositions from classical Islamic logic. The passage clarifies that disjunctives (munfasila) are characterized by opposition ('inad) between parts, which renders their order irrelevant and allows for more than two parts. Conjunctives (muttasila), by contrast, are ordered by connection (ittisal) and are limited to an antecedent and consequent. Understanding these properties is essential for correctly analyzing conditional statements in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the passage's main claim
The author states two differences between disjunctive and conjunctive conditionals: order indifference and the number of parts. Locate these claims in the Arabic text.
2
Understand the basis: positive relation
The 'positive relation' (al-nisba al-ijabiyya) between parts differs: in disjunctives it is opposition ('inad), in conjunctives it is connection (ittisal). This relation underlies both differences.
Opposition means mutual exclusivity; connection means the consequent follows from the antecedent.
3
Analyze the order indifference
In disjunctives, reversing the parts does not change meaning because of opposition. In conjunctives, reversing does change meaning, as shown by the example: 'If it is human, then it is animal' is true, but 'If it is animal, then it is human' is false. Animal is broader (a'amm) than human.
This demonstrates that conjunctives have a natural antecedent and consequent.
4
Examine the number of parts
Disjunctives can have two or more parts, either potentially infinite (e.g., 'the number is either two or three, etc.') or finite (e.g., 'the moving thing is either inanimate, plant, or animal'). Conjunctives have exactly two parts: antecedent and consequent. Any multiplicity is internal to the antecedent or consequent, not outside them.
For example, 'If this thing...' (the text cuts off, but the idea is that the antecedent may list multiple conditions but remains a single unit.)
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider a fiqh example: 'The act is either wajib or haram.' Swapping the parts gives 'The act is either haram or wajib' – same meaning. A conjunctive example: 'If the prayer is valid, then the ablution was correct' cannot be swapped without changing meaning. Also, a disjunctive can have more than two parts: 'The ruling is either wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, or haram.'
These properties are crucial for constructing valid disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا فرق في هذه المواضع بين أن تقدم في اللفظ ما أخرته أو تؤخر ما قدمته، إذ ليس لها مقدم وتال بالطبع لكون النسبة الإيجابية بين أجزائها عنادية بخلاف المتصلة إذ النسبة الإيجابية بين أجزائها بالاتصال، وربما كان أعم من المقدم كما في قولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان) فإنه وإن لزم الحيوان من وضع الإنسان، فلا يلزم الإنسان من وضع الحيوان، وربما اتفق التركيب في القضايا الشرطية مما ذكرناه من الموجبات الصرفة أو السوالب الصرفة أو خلط بعضها مع بعض فاعتبره بنفسك، لكن إن كانت القضية منفصلة جاز؛ لكون النسبة بين أجزائها بالعناد أن يكون تركيبها من جزأين فصاعداً، إما إلى غير النهاية لا بالقوة ولا بالفعل كما في قولنا: العدد إما اثنان وإما ثلاثة وهلم جرا)، وإما متناهية بالقوة والفعل كقولنا : المتحرك إما جماد وإما نبات وإما حيوان بخلاف المتصلة فإنها لا تكون أكثر من جزأين مقدم وتال والتكثر إن وقع فيها فليس خارجاً عن المقدم والتالي فإن كان التكثر في المقدم، فسواء كان الحكم بالتالي مما يصح على كل حال واحد من المتكثرات في المقدم كما في قولنا: إن كان هذا الشيء
And there is no difference in these topics between presenting in speech what you have delayed or delaying what you have presented, since it has no natural antecedent and consequent due to the positive relation between its parts being opposition, unlike the conjunctive, for the positive relation between its parts is by connection. And it may be more general than the antecedent, as in our saying: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal.' For even though animal follows from the positing of human, human does not follow from the positing of animal. And perhaps composition may occur in conditional propositions from what we have mentioned of pure affirmatives or pure negatives, or mixing some with each other, so consider that yourself. But if the proposition is disjunctive, it is permissible, due to the relation between its parts being opposition, that its composition be from two parts or more, either ad infinitum neither potentially nor actually, as in our saying: 'The number is either two or three, and so on,' or finite both potentially and actually, as in our saying: 'The moving thing is either inanimate, or plant, or animal.' Unlike the conjunctive, for it is not more than two parts: antecedent and consequent. And if multiplicity occurs in it, it is not outside the antecedent and consequent. If the multiplicity is in the antecedent, then whether the judgment by the consequent holds for every single one of the multiplicities in the antecedent, as in our saying: 'If this thing...'
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage clarifies two key differences between disjunctive (munfasila) and conjunctive (muttasila) propositions. First, regarding order: in disjunctives, there is no natural order of antecedent and consequent because the positive relation (al-nisba al-ijabiyya) between the parts is opposition ('inad). This means one can reorder the parts without changing the logical meaning—'either A or B' is equivalent to 'either B or A'. In contrast, conjunctives have a natural order because the relation is one of connection (ittisal); changing the order would alter the meaning, as shown by the example: 'If it is human, then it is animal' does not imply 'If it is animal, then it is human' because animal is a broader category (a'amm) than human. The text also notes that compound conditionals can mix affirmatives and negatives, leaving verification to the reader. Second, regarding the number of parts: a disjunctive can have two or more parts, either potentially infinite (e.g., 'the number is either two or three, etc.') or finite (e.g., 'the moving thing is either inanimate, plant, or animal'). A conjunctive, however, strictly has only two parts: antecedent and consequent. If multiplicity appears, it is not a third part but rather multiplicity within the antecedent or consequent themselves. For example, 'If this thing...' (the text cuts off, but the idea is that the antecedent might list multiple conditions, but the structure remains binary). This distinction is crucial for understanding the logical grammar of conditionals in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
النسبة الإيجابية (al-nisba al-ijabiyya): The positive relation between parts of a conditional; in conjunctives it is connection (ittisal), in disjunctives it is opposition ('inad). [Note: Stable; used to characterize the logical relationship.]
العناد (al-'inad): Opposition or incompatibility; the relation in disjunctive propositions where the parts are mutually exclusive. [Note: No significant shift.]
الاتصال (al-ittisal): Connection; the relation in conjunctive propositions where the consequent follows from the antecedent. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because disjunctives express opposition, allowing multiple alternatives.
B) Because conjunctives are limited by the laws of nature.
C) Because disjunctives are always about numbers.
D) Because the text does not give a reason.

Key Takeaway
Disjunctives are defined by opposition, allowing order flexibility and multiple parts; conjunctives are defined by connection, requiring a fixed antecedent-consequent structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the disjunctive proposition: 'Either the contract is valid or it is void.' Reword it by swapping the parts. Is the meaning changed? Explain using the concept of 'inad.
?
Identify whether each of the following is a conjunctive or disjunctive conditional: (a) 'If the witness testifies truthfully, then his testimony is accepted.' (b) 'The legal ruling is either halal or haram.' (c) 'The number is either even or odd.' For each, state whether the order of parts matters and how many parts it has.

Types of Composite Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates all combinations of conditional propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven possible combinations of conditional propositions (conjunctive, disjunctive, categorical) as outlined in the text, and determine the type of relation (ittisal or inad) in each.

Core Idea
In Islamic logic, conditional propositions can be nested, and the order of parts in disjunctives is irrelevant due to mutual exclusion.

In the previous lessons, we learned about simple conditionals — both conjunctive (if...then) and disjunctive (either...or). Now the author expands on this by showing how conditionals can themselves be components of larger conditionals. This passage is a comprehensive classification of all possible combinations, each illustrated with an example. By systematically working through these, you will gain a deep understanding of how conditionals can be structured in complex reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish conjunctive from disjunctive outer propositions
First, determine whether the overall conditional is a conjunctive (if...then) or a disjunctive (either...or). This is the outermost relation.
The text shows both cases.
2
Identify the components of the outer proposition
Look at what the outer proposition is composed of: it could be two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical and a disjunctive, etc. The text lists seven combinations.
Be systematic: consider both the type of the outer and the types of the inner propositions.
3
Check the relation type (ittisal vs. inad)
For conjunctive parts, the relation is connection (ittisal). For disjunctive parts, the relation is exclusion (inad). This determines whether order matters.
In disjunctives, there is no natural antecedent/consequent.
4
Recognize the generality principle
In a conjunctive, the consequent can be more general than the antecedent, as in 'If this is a human, then it is an animal.' But the reverse does not hold.
This is important for understanding the asymmetry of connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني: في القضايا الشرطية علة النهار، وإن كانت متصلة ومنفصلة فإما أن تكون المتصلة هي المقدم أو التالي فإن كانت مقدماً فكقولنا: إن كان كل ما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فإما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً، وإن كانت تالياً فكقولنا: إن كان إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً فكل ما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار (موجود) وأما المنفصلة فإن كانت من منفصلتين فكقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا الموجود جوهراً وإما عرضاً وإما أن يكون ليس بجوهر وإما ليس بعرض)، وإن كانت من متصلتين فكقولنا: إما أن يكون إذا كان العدد زوجاً فمنقسم بمتساويين وإما أن يكون إذا كان زوجاً غير منقسم بمتساويين)، وإن كانت من حملية ومنفصلة فكقولنا: (إما إما أن يكون هذا زوجاً وإما فرداً وإما أما أن لا يكون عدداً، وإن كانت من حَمْلية ومتصلة فكقولنا: إما أما أن تكون الشمس علة النهار وإما أن تكون كل ما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود، وإن كانت من متصلة ومنفصلة فكقولنا: (إما إما أن يكون كل ما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً)، ولا فرق في هذه المواضع بين أن تقدم في اللفظ ما أخرته أو تؤخر ما قدمته، إذ ليس لها مقدم وتال بالطبع لكون النسبة الإيجابية بين أجزائها عنادية بخلاف المتصلة إذ النسبة الإيجابية بين أجزائها بالاتصال، وربما كان أعم من المقدم كما في قولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان) فإنه وإن لزم الحيوان من وضع الإنسان، فلا يلزم الإنسان من وضع الحيوان، وربما اتفق التركيب في القضايا الشرطية مما ذكرناه من الموجبات الصرفة أو السوالب الصرفة أو خلط بعضها مع بعض فاعتبره بنفسك
The second art: On conditional propositions. The cause of daytime, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive: then the conjunctive is either the antecedent or the consequent. If it is an antecedent, as we say: 'If whenever the sun is rising then daytime exists, then either the sun is rising or night exists.' If it is a consequent, as we say: 'If either the sun is rising or night exists, then whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' As for the disjunctive: if it is from two disjunctives, as we say: 'Either this existent is a substance or an accident, and either it is not a substance or not an accident.' If from two conjunctives, as we say: 'Either if the number is even then it is divisible by equal parts, or if it is even then it is not divisible by equal parts.' If from a categorical and a disjunctive, as we say: 'Either this is even or odd, or else it is not a number.' If from a categorical and a conjunctive, as we say: 'Either the sun is the cause of daytime, or whenever the sun is rising, daytime does not exist.' If from a conjunctive and a disjunctive, as we say: 'Either whenever the sun is rising then daytime exists, or either the sun is rising or daytime exists.' There is no difference in these cases between preceding in speech what you have delayed or delaying what you have preceded, because it has no natural antecedent and consequent due to the positive relation between its parts being adversarial, unlike the conjunctive, for the positive relation between its parts is by connection. And it is sometimes more general than the antecedent, as in our saying: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal.' For even though the animal follows from positing the human, the human does not follow from positing the animal. And sometimes composition in conditional propositions occurs from what we have mentioned of pure affirmatives or pure negatives or mixing some with others, so consider it yourself.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the possible compositions of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya). The author first considers the case where the conditional proposition is itself a component in a larger conditional. He gives examples for each combination: (1) a conjunctive conditional as antecedent or consequent, (2) a disjunctive conditional combined with another disjunctive, (3) a disjunctive combined with a conjunctive, (4) a categorical with a disjunctive, (5) a categorical with a conjunctive, (6) a conjunctive with a disjunctive. He notes that the order of the components does not matter because in disjunctives the relation is one of mutual exclusion (inad), not connection (ittisal). He also notes that the antecedent and consequent are not inherently ordered, unlike in conditionals where the relation is connection. He then gives an example of a conditional where the consequent is more general than the antecedent. Finally, he says that these compositions can be made from pure affirmatives, pure negatives, or a mixture, and leaves the student to verify.

Key Terms
القضية الشرطية (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya): A proposition that expresses a conditional relationship between two parts (antecedent and consequent) either by connection (ittisal) or by exclusion (inad). [Note: Stable term across classical logic.]
متصلة (muttasila): A conditional proposition in the form 'If A then B', where the relation is one of necessary connection.
منفصلة (munfasila): A conditional proposition in the form 'Either A or B', where the relation is one of mutual exclusion (inad). [Note: In some later texts, munfasila is further divided into exclusive and inclusive, but here it is simply disjunctive.]
مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent in a conditional proposition, the part that is assumed or placed first.
تالي (tali): The consequent in a conditional proposition, the part that follows from the antecedent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Which of the following is a legitimate combination of conditional propositions according to the text?
A) A conjunctive conditional combined with a categorical proposition.
B) A disjunctive conditional combined with a categorical proposition.
C) Both A and B.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The classification of composite conditionals is exhaustive: any combination of conjunctive, disjunctive, and categorical propositions inside a conjunctive or disjunctive frame is possible, with the key distinction that disjunctives lack natural order.

Why Disjunctives Can Have Multiple Parts
Author distinguishes conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals regarding part multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a disjunctive conditional can have more than two parts while a conjunctive cannot, using the author's distinction about the adversarial relation.

Core Idea
Disjunctives allow multiple parts due to exclusion, not connection.

The author explains that the conjunctive conditional is structurally limited to an antecedent and a consequent because it expresses a conditional relation. In contrast, the disjunctive conditional is based on mutual exclusion (inad), which naturally allows multiple disjuncts. This lesson unpacks why that is and what it means for the proposition's unity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Find where the author distinguishes between muttasila and munfasila. Muttasila is the conjunctive conditional (if...then), munfasila is the disjunctive (either...or).
2
Find the reason for disjunctive multiplicity
The author says 'because the relation between its parts is adversarial (inad)' – this is the key. Inad means mutual exclusion, so there is no fixed order; any number of mutually exclusive options can be listed.
3
Contrast with conjunctive limitation
The author explicitly says the conjunctive 'is not more than two parts, an antecedent and a consequent.' This is because the relation is one of connection, requiring a clear condition and result.
4
Examine the examples
The author gives examples: numbers (potentially infinite) and divisions of moving things into three categories (finite). See how the disjunctive can list many options.
5
Formulate the principle
In your own words, explain: Disjunctives can have multiple parts because exclusion does not impose a limit, while conjunctions require a pair.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
