Key Takeaway
In conditional propositions, 'particularity' refers to the specification of the time or condition, not the quantity of the subject, unlike categorical propositions.

The Three Main Categories of Disjunctive Conditionals
Author classifies disjunctive conditionals into quantified, absolute, and particular types with sub-branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given disjunctive conditional as quantified (محصورة), absolute (مهملة), or particular (مخصوصة) based on the presence of a quantifier and temporal specificity.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals are classified by quantification and temporal specificity, not by the quantity of their parts.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of disjunctive conditionals into three types: quantified, absolute, and particular. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly analyzing conditional arguments in Islamic logic, as it determines how the proposition behaves in syllogisms and debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
Read the first sentence of the passage. The author states that a disjunctive conditional is either quantified (محصورة), absolute (مهملة), or particular (مخصوصة). This is the primary classification.
Quantified means it has an explicit quantifier (كل or بعض). Absolute means no quantifier. Particular means tied to a specific time.
2
Understand the quantified branch
If quantified, it further divides into universal (كلية) and particular (جزئية). Note that it does not stop at quantification—the universal can be affirmative or negative.
This shows that quantification is not the only division; the quality (affirmative/negative) also matters for universals.
3
Recognize the absolute type
The author says that if the disjunctive is absolute (مهملة), its ruling is the same as the particular. This means it is treated as a particular proposition in logical operations.
Absolute disjunctives lack a quantifier but are not ambiguous; they are equivalent to 'some' statements.
4
Recognize the particular type
The particular (مخصوصة) disjunctive is defined by a relation of opposition or negation at a specific time, as previously explained for conjunctive conditionals.
This type is time-specific, unlike the quantified types which are timeless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت منفصلة فإما محصورة أو مهملة أو مخصوصة، فإن كانت محصورة فإما كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة فهي ما النسبة بين جزئيها بالعناد والانفصال دائماً، وسواء كانت أجزاء القضية شخصية أو مهملة أو محصورة أو مختلطة، مثال الأول قولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً وإما أن لا يكون زوجاً)، ومثال الثاني قولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون العدد فرداً، ومثال الثالث إن كان الحصر كلياً قولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون كل إنسان ناطقاً وإما كل إنسان ليس ناطقاً، ومثاله إن كان الحصر جزئياً قولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون بعض الإنسان ناطقاً وإما ليس بناطق)، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى عليك الإيجاب الكلي فيها. وإن اختلفت الأجزاء وإن كانت المنفصلة سالبة كلية فهي ما دخل حرف السلب فيها على النسبة العنادية دائماً كقولنا: دائماً ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن لا يكون فرداً، وكذا في غيره من الضروب وإن كانت المنفصلة فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فهي ما النسبة العنادية بين أجزائها في بعض الأوقات، ولا تبال على أي حال كانت أجزاء المنفصلة من الخصوص والحصر والإهمال، واللفظة الدالة على ذلك ما كانت دالة على جزئية المتصلة كقولنا: (قد يكون إما العدد زوجاً وإما فرداً)، والسلب الجزئي فيها هو رفع هذه النسبة كقولنا: (ليس دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما لا فرداً وإن كانت مهملة فحكمها حكم الجزئية، ولا يخفى مثاله. وإن كانت مخصوصة فهي ما النسبة بين جزأيها بالعناد أو سالبة في وقت معین مخصوص كما بيناه في المتصلة، ولا يخفى مثاله.
And if it is disjunctive, then it is either quantified (maḥṣūra) or absolute (muhmala) or particular (mukhassaṣa). If it is quantified, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, it is that in which the relation between its two parts is of opposition and disjunction always, regardless of whether the parts of the proposition are singular, absolute, quantified, or mixed. The first example is our statement: 'Always, either this number is even or it is not even.' The second example is our statement: 'Always, either the number is even or the number is odd.' The third example, if the quantification is universal, is our statement: 'Always, either every human is rational or every human is not rational.' And its example, if the quantification is particular, is our statement: 'Always, either some human is rational or he is not rational.' Based on this, the universal affirmative in it is not hidden from you. And if the parts differ, and if the disjunctive is universal negative, it is that in which the negative particle enters upon the oppositional relation always, as our statement: 'Always, it is not that either the number is even or it is not odd.' And similarly for other modes. And the disjunctive is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, it is that in which the oppositional relation between its parts exists at some times, not caring in what state the parts of the disjunctive are, whether particularity, quantification, or absoluteness. The word indicating that is that which indicates the particularity of the conjunctive, as our statement: 'It may be that either the number is even or odd.' The particular negation in it is the removal of this relation, as our statement: 'Not always either the number is even or not odd.' And if it is absolute (muhmala), its ruling is the same as the particular; its example is not hidden. And if it is particular (mukhassaṣa), it is that in which the relation between its two parts is of opposition or negation at a specific, particular time, as we explained in the conjunctive; its example is not hidden.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage classifies disjunctive conditional propositions (المنفصلة) into three main categories: quantified (محصورة), absolute (مهملة), and particular (مخصوصة). The quantified further divides into universal and particular, each of which can be affirmative or negative. The author gives detailed examples for universal affirmative disjunctives, noting that the relation of opposition (عناد) and disjunction (انفصال) must hold always (دائماً). The examples cover cases where the parts are singular, absolute, quantified (universal or particular). He then explains universal negative disjunctives, where the negation applies to the oppositional relation itself. Next, he discusses particular disjunctives (both affirmative and negative), where the opposition holds only sometimes (في بعض الأوقات). He also notes that absolute disjunctives (مهملة) are treated like particular ones. Finally, he addresses particular (مخصوصة) disjunctives, where the opposition or negation is tied to a specific time. Throughout, the author emphasizes that the quantity and quality of the parts do not directly determine the quantity and quality of the whole disjunctive.

Universal Affirmative Disjunctives
Author classifies disjunctive conditionals into quantified, absolute, and particular types with sub-branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a universal affirmative disjunctive conditional, and verify that the opposition relation holds always.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative disjunctive demands that the opposition between its parts be always true.

This lesson delves into the universal affirmative disjunctive, where the relation of opposition (عناد) and disjunction (انفصال) holds at all times. The author provides several examples to clarify that this 'always' holds regardless of the quantification of the individual parts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence that begins 'فإن كانت كليّة موجبة فهي ما النسبة بين جزئيها بالعناد والانفصال دائماً'. This is the defining characteristic: the opposition is perpetual.
The word 'دائماً' (always) is the key qualifier.
2
Examine the examples
The author gives three examples with different part types: singular ('this number'), universal absolute ('the number' without quantifier), and quantified ('every human', 'some human'). Notice that in each case, the disjunctive is universal affirmative because the opposition holds always.
The parts can be singular, absolute, or quantified; that does not change the universality of the whole disjunctive.
3
Understand the condition
The author says 'Sawa' (سواء) meaning 'regardless of' the state of the parts. This emphasizes that the universality of the disjunctive comes from the 'always' in the opposition, not from the parts being universal.
This is a crucial point: the whole proposition's quantity depends on the temporal scope of the opposition, not on the quantifiers within the parts.
4
Apply the concept
Construct your own example: pick two mutually exclusive predicates for all times, like 'every human is either alive or dead' (دائماً كل إنسان إما حي وإما ميت). Verify that the opposition holds always.
Ensure that the two alternatives are truly exhaustive and mutually exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Universal Negative and Particular Disjunctives
Author classifies disjunctive conditionals into quantified, absolute, and particular types with sub-branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative disjunctives, and construct examples of each.

Core Idea
Universal negations apply to the opposition relation itself, while particular disjunctives restrict the opposition to some times.

This lesson covers the remaining types of disjunctive conditionals: universal negative and particular (affirmative and negative). The author explains that universal negatives place the negation on the oppositional relation, while particular disjunctives (both affirmative and negative) limit the opposition to some occasions. He also reiterates that absolute disjunctives are treated as particular.

Guided Path
1
Understand universal negative
Read the sentence: 'وإن كانت المنفصلة سالبة كلية فهي ما دخل حرف السلب فيها على النسبة العنادية دائماً'. The negation (حرف السلب) applies to the oppositional relation itself, not to the parts. Example: 'Always, it is not that either the number is even or it is not odd.' This means it is never the case that the two options hold together? Actually, it negates the disjunction itself: it's not always that either... or...
The negation is on the entire disjunction, not on the individual parts.
2
Examine particular affirmative
Read: 'فإن كانت موجبة فهي ما النسبة العنادية بين أجزائها في بعض الأوقات'. The opposition holds only at some times (في بعض الأوقات). Example: 'It may be that either the number is even or odd.' This is particular affirmative.
The word 'قد' (may) or 'بعض' indicates particularity.
3
Understand particular negative
The particular negative is the removal of the opposition relation at all times: 'ليس دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما لا فرداً' (Not always either the number is even or not odd). This means there is at least one time when the opposition does not hold.
Particular negative is equivalent to 'not (universal affirmative)'. It does not assert a specific instance, only denies the universal claim.
4
Note the absolute type
The author states that absolute (مهملة) disjunctives are treated like particular ones. So they follow the same rules as particular disjunctives.
This simplifies the classification: absolute are not a separate logical category; they are assimilated to particular.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت منفصلة فإما محصورة أو مهملة أو مخصوصة، فإن كانت محصورة فإما كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة فهي ما النسبة بين جزئيها بالعناد والانفصال دائماً، وسواء كانت أجزاء القضية شخصية أو مهملة أو محصورة أو مختلطة، مثال الأول قولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً وإما أن لا يكون زوجاً)، ومثال الثاني قولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون العدد فرداً، ومثال الثالث إن كان الحصر كلياً قولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون كل إنسان ناطقاً وإما كل إنسان ليس ناطقاً، ومثاله إن كان الحصر جزئياً قولنا: (دائماً إما أن يكون بعض الإنسان ناطقاً وإما ليس بناطق)، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى عليك الإيجاب الكلي فيها. وإن اختلفت الأجزاء وإن كانت المنفصلة سالبة كلية فهي ما دخل حرف السلب فيها على النسبة العنادية دائماً كقولنا: دائماً ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن لا يكون فرداً، وكذا في غيره من الضروب وإن كانت المنفصلة فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فهي ما النسبة العنادية بين أجزائها في بعض الأوقات، ولا تبال على أي حال كانت أجزاء المنفصلة من الخصوص والحصر والإهمال، واللفظة الدالة على ذلك ما كانت دالة على جزئية المتصلة كقولنا: (قد يكون إما العدد زوجاً وإما فرداً)، والسلب الجزئي فيها هو رفع هذه النسبة كقولنا: (ليس دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما لا فرداً وإن كانت مهملة فحكمها حكم الجزئية، ولا يخفى مثاله. وإن كانت مخصوصة فهي ما النسبة بين جزأيها بالعناد أو سالبة في وقت معین مخصوص كما بيناه في المتصلة، ولا يخفى مثاله.
And if it is disjunctive, then it is either quantified (maḥṣūra) or absolute (muhmala) or particular (mukhassaṣa). If it is quantified, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, it is that in which the relation between its two parts is of opposition and disjunction always, regardless of whether the parts of the proposition are singular, absolute, quantified, or mixed. The first example is our statement: 'Always, either this number is even or it is not even.' The second example is our statement: 'Always, either the number is even or the number is odd.' The third example, if the quantification is universal, is our statement: 'Always, either every human is rational or every human is not rational.' And its example, if the quantification is particular, is our statement: 'Always, either some human is rational or he is not rational.' Based on this, the universal affirmative in it is not hidden from you. And if the parts differ, and if the disjunctive is universal negative, it is that in which the negative particle enters upon the oppositional relation always, as our statement: 'Always, it is not that either the number is even or it is not odd.' And similarly for other modes. And the disjunctive is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, it is that in which the oppositional relation between its parts exists at some times, not caring in what state the parts of the disjunctive are, whether particularity, quantification, or absoluteness. The word indicating that is that which indicates the particularity of the conjunctive, as our statement: 'It may be that either the number is even or odd.' The particular negation in it is the removal of this relation, as our statement: 'Not always either the number is even or not odd.' And if it is absolute (muhmala), its ruling is the same as the particular; its example is not hidden. And if it is particular (mukhassaṣa), it is that in which the relation between its two parts is of opposition or negation at a specific, particular time, as we explained in the conjunctive; its example is not hidden.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage classifies disjunctive conditional propositions (المنفصلة) into three main categories: quantified (محصورة), absolute (مهملة), and particular (مخصوصة). The quantified further divides into universal and particular, each of which can be affirmative or negative. The author gives detailed examples for universal affirmative disjunctives, noting that the relation of opposition (عناد) and disjunction (انفصال) must hold always (دائماً). The examples cover cases where the parts are singular, absolute, quantified (universal or particular). He then explains universal negative disjunctives, where the negation applies to the oppositional relation itself. Next, he discusses particular disjunctives (both affirmative and negative), where the opposition holds only sometimes (في بعض الأوقات). He also notes that absolute disjunctives (مهملة) are treated like particular ones. Finally, he addresses particular (مخصوصة) disjunctives, where the opposition or negation is tied to a specific time. Throughout, the author emphasizes that the quantity and quality of the parts do not directly determine the quantity and quality of the whole disjunctive.

منفصلة جزئية موجبة
منفصلة جزئية سالبة
في بعض الأوقات

Universal negative disjunctives negate the opposition relation always, while particular disjunctives affirm or negate it at some times.

Affirmation and Negation of Conditionals: The Relation Matters
Author explains that affirmation/negation follows the relation, not the parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional proposition is affirmative or negative by examining the relation between its parts, not the quality of the parts.

Core Idea
The relation, not the parts, determines a conditional’s affirmation or negation.

Many students mistakenly think that a conditional is affirmative only if all its parts are affirmative, and negative if any part is negative. In this passage, the author corrects this error by showing that affirmation and negation apply to the relation (concomitance or opposition), not to the parts themselves. Through examples from conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, you will learn to focus on the logical connection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of the conditional
For a conjunctive conditional (متصلة), locate the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي). For a disjunctive (منفصلة), identify the two disjuncts. Write them down.
2
Check the quality of each part (affirmative/negative)
Note whether each part is affirmative or negative. In the author's example 'If the sun is rising, then daytime is not existing', both parts are negative. In 'Either the number is not even or it is not odd', both disjuncts are negative.
3
Examine the relation between the parts
In a conjunctive, the relation is either concomitance (ملازمة) or connection (اتصال); if the relation holds, the conditional is affirmative. In a disjunctive, the relation is opposition (عناد); if the disjunction holds, it is affirmative. If the relation is explicitly negated (رفع الاتصال or رفع الانفصال), the conditional is negative.
The author's example of a negative conditional is 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then daytime is existing' — here the negation applies to the connection, not to the parts.
4
Determine affirmation or negation based on the relation
If the relation (concomitance or opposition) exists, the conditional is affirmative (موجبة). If the relation is negated, the conditional is negative (سالبة). The quality of the parts is irrelevant.
Practice: 'If the traveler is not ill, then he must fast' is affirmative because the relation (rule) holds, even though the antecedent is negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما أنه ليس إهمال المنفصلة الشرطية وخصوصها وحصرها بخصوص للأجزاء وحصرها وإهمالها، فليس إيجاب القضية الشرطية وسلبها لكون أجزائها أو بعض أجزائها موجبة أو سالبة، ولا لكونها مطلقة أو موجهة لكون أجزائها مطلقة أو موجهة، ولا كذبها وصدقها، وكثير مما يغلط في ذلك. أما الأول فكثير ما تكون الأجزاء أو بعضها سوالب والقضية موجبة، أو موجبة والقضية سالبة، مثال الأول من المتصلة قولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود فإنه وإن كان كل واحد من جزأيها سالباً فالنسبة بينهما بالملازمة والاتصال، وهو معنى الإيجاب فيها، ومثاله من إما أن لا يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن لا يكون فرداً، فإنه وإن كان كل واحد من جزأيها أيضاً سالباً فالنسبة بينهما بالعناد وهو معنى الإيجاب في المنفصلة، ومثال الثاني من المتصلة قولنا : ليس) إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود ومثاله من المتصلة ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما فردا فإنه وإن كان كل واحد من أجزائها موجباً فالنسبة بين أجزاء المنفصلة برفع الانفصال، وفي المتصلة برفع الاتصال، وهو معنى السلب فيهما، وأما الثاني فلأنه قد تكون القضية الشرطية مطلقة أي أنه لم يذكر معها لفظ يدل على مادة القضية أي حال أحد الجزأين بالنسبة إلى الآخر، وإن كانت الأجزاء موجهة، وقد تكون موجهة أي قد صرح فيها باللفظ الدال على حال أحد الجزأين بالنسبة إلى الآخر من الضرورة والإمكان أو الامتناع، وإن كانت الأجزاء مطلقة، فإن كانت القضية مطلقة والأجزاء موجهة فإما أن تكون ضرورية صرفة أو ممكنة صرفة أو مختلطة، مثال الأول من المتصلة قولنا: (بالإطلاق إن كان "ب" "أ" بالضرورة فـ "ج" "د" بالضرورة أي "ج" "د" بالضرورة لازم بالإطلاق لما هو "ب" "أ" بالضرورة ومثاله من المنفصلة قولنا: (بالإطلاق إما أن يكون "ب" "أ" بالضرورة وإما أن يكون "ج" "د" بالضرورة)، ومثال الثاني من المتصلة قولنا : بالإطلاق) إن كان "ب" "أ" بالإمكان فـ "ج" "د" بالإمكان)، ومثاله من المنفصلة قولنا بالإطلاق) إما أن يكون "ب" "أ" بالإمكان وإما بالإمكان لا يكون ب" "أ")، ومثال الثالث من المتصلة قولنا بالإطلاق) إن كان "ب" "أ" بالضرورة فـ "ج" "د" بالإمكان ومثاله من المنفصلة قولنا : بالإطلاق إما أن يكون "ب" "أ"
And just as there is absoluteness, particularity, and quantification of the disjunctive conditional [proposition] due to the particularity, quantification, and absoluteness of the parts, so the affirmation and negation of the conditional proposition is not due to its parts or some of its parts being affirmative or negative, nor due to its being absolute or modalized because its parts are absolute or modalized, nor [is it] its falsehood and truth — and many err in that. As for the first: many times the parts or some of them are negative and the proposition is affirmative, or affirmative and the proposition is negative. The first example from the conjunctive is our statement: 'If the sun is rising, then the daytime is not existing'. Although each of its two parts is negative, the relation between them is of concomitance and connection, and that is the meaning of affirmation in it. And its example from [the disjunctive] is: 'Either the number is not even or it is not odd'. Although each of its two parts is also negative, the relation between them is opposition, and that is the meaning of affirmation in the disjunctive. And the second example from the conjunctive is our statement: 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then daytime is existing'. And its example from the disjunctive is: 'It is not that either the number is even or odd'. Although each of its parts is affirmative, the relation between the parts of the disjunctive is lifting the disjunction, and in the conjunctive it is lifting the connection, and that is the meaning of negation in both. As for the second: the conditional proposition may be absolute, i.e., no word indicating the matter of the proposition — i.e., the state of one part relative to the other — is mentioned, even if the parts are modalized; and it may be modalized, i.e., the word indicating the state of one part relative to the other — of necessity, possibility, or impossibility — is explicitly stated, even if the parts are absolute. If the proposition is absolute and the parts are modalized, then either [the proposition] is purely necessary, purely possible, or mixed. The first example from the conjunctive is our statement: 'Absolutely, if B is A necessarily, then J is D necessarily' — i.e., 'J is D necessarily is an absolute concomitant of what is B is A necessarily.' And its example from the disjunctive is our statement: 'Absolutely, either B is A necessarily or J is D necessarily.' And the second example from the conjunctive is our statement: 'Absolutely, if B is A possibly, then J is D possibly.' And its example from the disjunctive is our statement: 'Absolutely, either B is A possibly or it is possible that B is not A.' And the third example from the conjunctive is our statement: 'Absolutely, if B is A necessarily, then J is D possibly.' And its example from the disjunctive is our statement: 'Absolutely, either B is A...'
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage addresses two common errors regarding conditional propositions. First, the affirmation or negation of the conditional is independent of the affirmation or negation of its parts. The author provides examples: a conjunctive conditional with two negative parts can still be affirmative, as in 'If the sun is rising, then daytime is not existing' — the relation is one of concomitance (ملازمة), which constitutes affirmation. Similarly, a disjunctive with two negative parts ('Either the number is not even or it is not odd') is affirmative because the relation is opposition (عناد). Conversely, a conditional with affirmative parts can be negative, as in 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then daytime is existing' — here the negation applies to the relation, not the parts. The second error concerns modality: a conditional proposition can be absolute (مطلقة) — having no modal modifier — even if its parts are modalized; conversely, it can be modalized (موجهة) even if its parts are absolute. The author then categorizes absolute conditionals with modalized parts into three types: purely necessary, purely possible, and mixed. Examples are given using variable notation (ب، أ, etc.) for clarity. The overall lesson is that the logical form of a conditional is determined by the relation between its parts, not by the internal quality or modality of the parts.

إيجاب
سلب
متصل
منفصل
عناد
ملازمة

C) 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then daytime is existing' (negation of relation).
D) 'If the number is even, then it is not odd' (negative consequent).

Key Takeaway
In conditional propositions, affirmation and negation belong to the relation (concomitance/opposition), not to the parts.

Absolute vs Modalized Conditionals: Modality Belongs to the Relation
Author contrasts absolute vs modalized conditionals and their relation to parts' modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional propositions as absolute or modalized based on whether the relation itself carries a modal word, and identify the three types of absolute conditionals with modalized parts.

Core Idea
Modality in conditionals attaches to the relation, not to the parts, and absolute conditionals can have modalized parts.

The author now addresses a second error: thinking that if the parts of a conditional are modalized (e.g., necessary, possible), then the conditional itself is modalized. He explains that modality pertains to the relation itself. A conditional is absolute (مطلقة) if no modal word is attached to the relation, regardless of the modality of its parts. Conversely, a conditional can be modalized (موجهة) even if its parts are absolute. He then categorizes absolute conditionals with modalized parts into three types: purely necessary, purely possible, and mixed.

Guided Path
1
Look for a modal word attached to the relation
In a conditional, check if there is a word like 'necessarily' (بالضرورة), 'possibly' (بالإمكان), or 'impossible' (بالامتناع) that describes the link between antecedent and consequent. If such a word appears, the conditional is modalized (موجهة). If not, it is absolute (مطلقة).
2
Check the modality of the parts
Even if the parts themselves contain modal terms (e.g., 'B is A necessarily'), this does not make the conditional modalized. The modality of the parts is separate from the modality of the relation.
3
If the conditional is absolute but the parts are modalized, categorize the type
The author lists three subtypes: (1) purely necessary – both parts are necessary; (2) purely possible – both parts are possible; (3) mixed – one part necessary and the other possible.
The author gives examples using variables: 'Absolutely, if B is A necessarily, then J is D necessarily' (purely necessary); 'Absolutely, if B is A possibly, then J is D possibly' (purely possible); 'Absolutely, if B is A necessarily, then J is D possibly' (mixed).
4
Understand that the same applies to disjunctive conditionals
The same rules hold for disjunctives: they can be absolute or modalized, and if absolute with modalized disjuncts, they fall into the same three categories.
Example: 'Absolutely, either B is A necessarily or J is D necessarily' is an absolute disjunctive with necessary parts.

Commentary
This passage addresses two common errors regarding conditional propositions. First, the affirmation or negation of the conditional is independent of the affirmation or negation of its parts. The author provides examples: a conjunctive conditional with two negative parts can still be affirmative, as in 'If the sun is rising, then daytime is not existing' — the relation is one of concomitance (ملازمة), which constitutes affirmation. Similarly, a disjunctive with two negative parts ('Either the number is not even or it is not odd') is affirmative because the relation is opposition (عناد). Conversely, a conditional with affirmative parts can be negative, as in 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then daytime is existing' — here the negation applies to the relation, not the parts. The second error concerns modality: a conditional proposition can be absolute (مطلقة) — having no modal modifier — even if its parts are modalized; conversely, it can be modalized (موجهة) even if its parts are absolute. The author then categorizes absolute conditionals with modalized parts into three types: purely necessary, purely possible, and mixed. Examples are given using variable notation (ب، أ, etc.) for clarity. The overall lesson is that the logical form of a conditional is determined by the relation between its parts, not by the internal quality or modality of the parts.

Key Terms
مطلقة
موجهة
ضرورة
إمكان
امتناع
صرفة
مختلطة

A) 'Necessarily, if the sun rises, then it is day.'
B) 'If the sun rises, then it is necessarily day.'
C) 'If it is possible that the sun rises, then it is possible that it is day.'
D) 'If the sun rises, then it is day.'

Key Takeaway
A conditional's modality is determined by the relation, not by the parts; absolute conditionals with modalized parts fall into three subtypes: purely necessary, purely possible, or mixed.

Affirmation and Negation of Conditionals Depend on Relation, Not Parts
Author explains first property: affirmation/negation independent of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional proposition is affirmative or negative based on its relational content, not the polarity of its parts.

Core Idea
A conditional's affirmation is about the relation, not its parts.

Many students mistakenly think that if a conditional has negative parts, it must be negative. This lesson clarifies that the affirmation or negation of a conditional—conjunctive or disjunctive—is determined by whether the relation (implication or opposition) is asserted or denied. We will examine examples from the text to internalize this principle.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the parts from the relation
A conditional has two parts (e.g., antecedent and consequent) and a relation (talazum or inad). The relation is what is affirmed or denied.
2
Identify the relation type
For conjunctive (muttasila), the relation is implication; for disjunctive (munfasila), it is opposition.
3
Check if the relation is asserted
If the relation is present (even with negative parts), the proposition is affirmative. If the relation is negated (even with positive parts), it is negative.
4
Apply to examples from the text
The text gives two affirmative examples (both parts negative) and two negative examples (both parts positive). Verify each using the rule.

The separate [disjunctive] conditional and its specifics and its restriction with respect to the parts and its restriction and its absoluteness. It is not that the affirmation of the conditional proposition and its negation is due to its parts or some of its parts being affirmative or negative, nor due to it being absolute or modal because its parts are absolute or modal, nor [due to] its falsehood and truth, and many err in that. As for the first, many times the parts or some of them are negatives and the proposition is affirmative, or affirmative and the proposition is negative. An example of the first from the conjunctive is our saying: 'If the sun is rising then the day does not exist.' For even though each of its two parts is negative, the relation between them is of implication and connection, and that is the meaning of affirmation in it. And an example of it from the disjunctive is our saying: 'Either the number is not even or it is not odd.' For even though each of its two parts is also negative, the relation between them is of opposition, and that is the meaning of affirmation in the disjunctive. And an example of the second from the conjunctive is our saying: 'It is not that if the sun is rising then the day exists.' And an example from the disjunctive is our saying: 'It is not that either the number is even or odd.' For even though each of its parts is affirmative, the relation between the parts of the disjunctive is the removal of disjunction, and in the conjunctive the removal of connection, and that is the meaning of negation in both. As for the second, it is because the conditional proposition may be absolute, i.e., no term was mentioned with it indicating the matter of the proposition, i.e., the state of one of the two parts with respect to the other, even if the parts are modal; and it may be modal, i.e., the term indicating the state of one of the two parts with respect to the other is explicitly mentioned, such as necessity, possibility, or impossibility, even if the parts are absolute. If the proposition is absolute and the parts are modal, then either they are purely necessary, purely possible, or mixed. An example of the first from the conjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'If "B" is necessarily "A" then "J" is necessarily "D";' i.e., "J" is necessarily "D" is an absolute necessary concomitant of what is "B" necessarily "A". And its example from the disjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'Either "B" is necessarily "A" or "J" is necessarily "D".' An example of the second from the conjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'If "B" is possibly "A" then "J" is possibly "D".' And its example from the disjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'Either "B" is possibly "A" or it is possibly not "B" is "A".' An example of the third from the conjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'If "B" is necessarily "A" then "J" is possibly "D".' And its example from the disjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'Either "B" is necessarily "A" or it is [possibly]' And if the proposition is modal and the parts are absolute, then an example from the conjunctive is our saying (necessarily or possibly): 'If "B" is "A" then "J" is "D"'; and from the disjunctive our saying (necessarily or possibly): 'Either "B" is "A" or "J" is "D".' As for the third, it is because the proposition may be true while its parts are false, and it may be false while its parts are true, as we have explained before.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage clarifies common errors about conditional propositions (shartiyya), which come in two types: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). The author addresses three misconceptions. First, the affirmation or negation of a conditional is not determined by whether its parts are affirmative or negative. For example, a conjunctive like 'If the sun is rising, then the day does not exist' has both parts negative, yet the proposition as a whole is affirmative because the relation (nisba) of implication (talazum) holds. Similarly, a disjunctive 'Either the number is not even or it is not odd' is affirmative due to opposition (inad). Conversely, a conditional can be negative even if its parts are affirmative, as in 'It is not that if the sun is rising then the day exists'. Second, the modality (madda) of a conditional—whether it is absolute (mutlaqa) or modal (muwajjaha)—is independent of the modality of its parts. A conditional may be absolute while its parts are modal, or modal while its parts are absolute. Examples are given with term variables (ب، ج، أ) to illustrate pure necessity, pure possibility, and mixed cases. Third, the truth of a conditional does not depend on the truth of its parts: a conditional can be true with false parts or false with true parts, as previously explained.

مُتَّصِلَة
مُنفَصِلَة

D) 'Either the sun is rising or the day exists' (disjunctive with two affirmative parts)

Key Takeaway
The affirmation or negation of a conditional is a property of the relation between its parts, not of the parts themselves.

The Modality of a Conditional is Independent of Its Parts
Author explains second property: modality of conditional independent of parts' modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and modal conditionals and explain how the modality of a conditional is independent of the modality of its parts.

Core Idea
A conditional can be absolute even if its parts are modal, and modal even if its parts are absolute.

In this lesson, we explore the second property of conditionals: modality. The author explains that a conditional proposition may carry a modality (necessity, possibility) as a whole, even if its parts express different modalities, or it may be absolute (unmodalized) while its parts are modal. We will see examples with variables to clarify this independence.

Guided Path
1
Understand absolute vs modal
Absolute (mutlaqa) means no modality word is attached to the conditional; modal (muwajjaha) means an explicit modality term (e.g., necessarily, possibly) is attached to the whole conditional.
2
Identify the modality of the parts
The parts themselves may be modal (e.g., 'B is necessarily A') or absolute ('B is A' with no modality).
3
Determine the modality of the conditional
The conditional's modality is expressed at the level of the whole proposition, not derived from the parts. The author emphasizes that a conditional can be absolute even if its parts are modal, and vice versa.
4
Examine the four combinations
The text outlines four cases: conditional absolute with parts modal (purely necessary, purely possible, or mixed), and conditional modal with parts absolute. Follow the examples given with variables.

The separate [disjunctive] conditional and its specifics and its restriction with respect to the parts and its restriction and its absoluteness. It is not that the affirmation of the conditional proposition and its negation is due to its parts or some of its parts being affirmative or negative, nor due to it being absolute or modal because its parts are absolute or modal, nor [due to] its falsehood and truth, and many err in that. The Truth of a Conditional is Independent of Its Parts' Truth
Author explains third property: truth of conditional independent of parts' truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify that a conditional's truth value is not determined by the truth of its parts, and produce examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A conditional can be true even if its parts are false, and false even if its parts are true.

The third important property of conditionals is that their truth is based on the relation (implication or opposition) holding, not on the truth of the individual parts. This lesson examines this principle and provides examples from the text to solidify your understanding.

Guided Path
1
Recall the truth condition for conditionals
A conjunctive conditional is true iff the antecedent actually implies the consequent (i.e., it is not the case that the antecedent is true and consequent false). A disjunctive is true depending on the type of disjunction (e.g., exclusive or inclusive).
2
Test with false parts
If both parts are false, the conditional can still be true if the implication holds vacuously (e.g., false antecedent implies anything). Similarly, for disjunctive, if one part is true the whole can be true, but the author notes that truth is independent.
3
Use the text's example
The author says 'the proposition may be true while its parts are false, and false while its parts are true.' Since he says 'as we have explained before', we infer examples like 'If humans are stones, then they are animals' (true because antecedent false).
4
Apply to Islamic proofs
In Kalam, a conditional like 'If the Qur'an is created, then it is contingent' is true regardless of the actual truth of the parts, as long as the implication holds.

المنفصلة الشرطية وخصوصها وحصرها بخصوص للأجزاء وحصرها وإهمالها، فليس إيجاب القضية الشرطية وسلبها لكون أجزائها أو بعض أجزائها موجبة أو سالبة، ولا لكونها مطلقة أو موجهة لكون أجزائها مطلقة أو موجهة، ولا كذبها وصدقها، وكثير مما يغلط في ذلك. أما الأول فكثير ما تكون الأجزاء أو بعضها سوالب والقضية موجبة، أو موجبة والقضية سالبة، مثال الأول من المتصلة قولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود فإنه وإن كان كل واحد من جزأيها سالباً فالنسبة بينهما بالملازمة والاتصال، وهو معنى الإيجاب فيها، ومثاله من إما أن لا يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن لا يكون فرداً، فإنه وإن كان كل واحد من جزأيها أيضاً سالباً فالنسبة بينهما بالعناد وهو معنى الإيجاب في المنفصلة، ومثال الثاني من المتصلة قولنا : ليس) إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود ومثاله من المتصلة ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما فردا فإنه وإن كان كل واحد من أجزائها موجباً فالنسبة بين أجزاء المنفصلة برفع الانفصال، وفي المتصلة برفع الاتصال، وهو معنى السلب فيهما، وأما الثاني فلأنه قد تكون القضية الشرطية مطلقة أي أنه لم يذكر معها لفظ يدل على مادة القضية أي حال أحد الجزأين بالنسبة إلى الآخر، وإن كانت الأجزاء موجهة، وقد تكون موجهة أي قد صرح فيها باللفظ الدال على حال أحد الجزأين بالنسبة إلى الآخر من الضرورة والإمكان أو الامتناع، وإن كانت الأجزاء مطلقة، فإن كانت القضية مطلقة والأجزاء موجهة فإما أن تكون ضرورية صرفة أو ممكنة صرفة أو مختلطة، مثال الأول من المتصلة قولنا: (بالإطلاق إن كان "ب" "أ" بالضرورة فـ "ج" "د" بالضرورة أي "ج" "د" بالضرورة لازم بالإطلاق لما هو "ب" "أ" بالضرورة ومثاله من المنفصلة قولنا: (بالإطلاق إما أن يكون "ب" "أ" بالضرورة وإما أن يكون "ج" "د" بالضرورة)، ومثال الثاني من المتصلة قولنا : بالإطلاق) إن كان "ب" "أ" بالإمكان فـ "ج" "د" بالإمكان)، ومثاله من المنفصلة قولنا بالإطلاق) إما أن يكون "ب" "أ" بالإمكان وإما بالإمكان لا يكون ب" "أ")، ومثال الثالث من المتصلة قولنا بالإطلاق) إن كان "ب" "أ" بالضرورة فـ "ج" "د" بالإمكان ومثاله من المنفصلة قولنا : بالإطلاق إما أن يكون "ب" "أ" بالضرورة وإما أن يكون بالإمكان بالضرورة وإما أن يكون بالإمكان وأما إن كانت القضية موجهة والأجزاء مطلقة فمثاله من المتصلة قولنا بالضرورة أو بالإمكان إن كان "ب" "أ" فـ "ج" "د")، ومن المنفصلة قولنا : بالضرورة أو الإمكان إما أن يكون "ب" "أ" وإما "ج" "د")، وأما الثالث فلأنه قد تكون القضية صادقة والأجزاء كاذبة، وقد تكون كاذبة والأجزاء صادقة كما بيناه من قبل
The separate [disjunctive] conditional and its specifics and its restriction with respect to the parts and its restriction and its absoluteness. It is not that the affirmation of the conditional proposition and its negation is due to its parts or some of its parts being affirmative or negative, nor due to it being absolute or modal because its parts are absolute or modal, nor [due to] its falsehood and truth, and many err in that. As for the first, many times the parts or some of them are negatives and the proposition is affirmative, or affirmative and the proposition is negative. An example of the first from the conjunctive is our saying: 'If the sun is rising then the day does not exist.' For even though each of its two parts is negative, the relation between them is of implication and connection, and that is the meaning of affirmation in it. And an example of it from the disjunctive is our saying: 'Either the number is not even or it is not odd.' For even though each of its two parts is also negative, the relation between them is of opposition, and that is the meaning of affirmation in the disjunctive. And an example of the second from the conjunctive is our saying: 'It is not that if the sun is rising then the day exists.' And an example from the disjunctive is our saying: 'It is not that either the number is even or odd.' For even though each of its parts is affirmative, the relation between the parts of the disjunctive is the removal of disjunction, and in the conjunctive the removal of connection, and that is the meaning of negation in both. As for the second, it is because the conditional proposition may be absolute, i.e., no term was mentioned with it indicating the matter of the proposition, i.e., the state of one of the two parts with respect to the other, even if the parts are modal; and it may be modal, i.e., the term indicating the state of one of the two parts with respect to the other is explicitly mentioned, such as necessity, possibility, or impossibility, even if the parts are absolute. If the proposition is absolute and the parts are modal, then either they are purely necessary, purely possible, or mixed. An example of the first from the conjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'If "B" is necessarily "A" then "J" is necessarily "D";' i.e., "J" is necessarily "D" is an absolute necessary concomitant of what is "B" necessarily "A". And its example from the disjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'Either "B" is necessarily "A" or "J" is necessarily "D".' An example of the second from the conjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'If "B" is possibly "A" then "J" is possibly "D".' And its example from the disjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'Either "B" is possibly "A" or it is possibly not "B" is "A".' An example of the third from the conjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'If "B" is necessarily "A" then "J" is possibly "D".' And its example from the disjunctive is our saying (absolutely): 'Either "B" is necessarily "A" or it is [possibly]' And if the proposition is modal and the parts are absolute, then an example from the conjunctive is our saying (necessarily or possibly): 'If "B" is "A" then "J" is "D"'; and from the disjunctive our saying (necessarily or possibly): 'Either "B" is "A" or "J" is "D".' As for the third, it is because the proposition may be true while its parts are false, and it may be false while its parts are true, as we have explained before.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage clarifies common errors about conditional propositions (shartiyya), which come in two types: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). The author addresses three misconceptions. First, the affirmation or negation of a conditional is not determined by whether its parts are affirmative or negative. For example, a conjunctive like 'If the sun is rising, then the day does not exist' has both parts negative, yet the proposition as a whole is affirmative because the relation (nisba) of implication (talazum) holds. Similarly, a disjunctive 'Either the number is not even or it is not odd' is affirmative due to opposition (inad). Conversely, a conditional can be negative even if its parts are affirmative, as in 'It is not that if the sun is rising then the day exists'. Second, the modality (madda) of a conditional—whether it is absolute (mutlaqa) or modal (muwajjaha)—is independent of the modality of its parts. A conditional may be absolute while its parts are modal, or modal while its parts are absolute. Examples are given with term variables (ب، ج، أ) to illustrate pure necessity, pure possibility, and mixed cases. Third, the truth of a conditional does not depend on the truth of its parts: a conditional can be true with false parts or false with true parts, as previously explained.

Key Terms
شَرطِيّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best illustrates that a conditional can be true even if its parts are false?
A) 'If the sun is rising, then the day exists' (both true)
B) 'If human beings are stones, then they are animals' (both false, implication holds vacuously)
C) 'If the number is even, then it is not odd' (one true, one false)
D) 'If the day exists, then the sun is rising' (both true but implication reversed? Actually that's false if day exists without sun, e.g., at night with artificial light)

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conditional is determined by the relation between its parts, not by the actual truth of the parts.

Converting Conditional Propositions
Author explains conversion of positive to negative conditionals and vice versa with proof by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert an affirmative conditional proposition into its negative equivalent (and vice versa) while preserving quantity and handling the case of a more general consequent.

Core Idea
Converting conditionals changes quality but preserves quantity and one part's contradictory.

In this passage, the author extends the concept of conversion (qalb) from categorical propositions to conditionals. You already know how to convert 'every A is B' to 'no A is B'. Now learn how to convert 'if A then B' to 'if A then not B' (and vice versa) under specific conditions. This is a powerful tool for immediate inference in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine if the proposition is connected (muttasila: 'if A then B') or disconnected (munfasila: 'either A or B').
The conversion rules apply differently; here we focus on connected conditionals.
2
Determine quality and quantity
Note whether the conditional is affirmative or negative, and whether it is universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya). Quantity must be preserved.
E.g., 'If A then B' is affirmative; 'If A then not B' is negative.
3
Identify antecedent and consequent
For a connected conditional, identify the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). In the conversion, one of these will be replaced by its contradictory.
In 'if A then B', A is antecedent, B is consequent.
4
Check generality of consequent
If the proposition is affirmative and the consequent is more general than the antecedent, the replacement must be the contradictory of the consequent, not the antecedent.
Example: 'If human then animal' – animal is more general, so the conversion takes the contradictory of animal, not human.
5
Perform the conversion
Change the quality (affirmative to negative or vice versa), keep the same quantity, and replace one part by its contradictory (following the rule above).
The result is logically equivalent and can be proven by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالضرورة وإما أن يكون بالإمكان وأما إن كانت القضية موجهة والأجزاء مطلقة فمثاله من المتصلة قولنا بالضرورة أو بالإمكان إن كان "ب" "أ" فـ "ج" "د")، ومن المنفصلة قولنا : بالضرورة أو الإمكان إما أن يكون "ب" "أ" وإما "ج" "د")، وأما الثالث فلأنه قد تكون القضية صادقة والأجزاء كاذبة، وقد تكون كاذبة والأجزاء صادقة كما بيناه من قبل، وإذ عرفت الشرطيتين وكيفياتها وكمياتها والمتضادة منها والداخلة تحت المتضادة على نحو ما عرفت في الحَمْليات، فاعلم أنه قد يصح قلب الموجبة من كل واحدة منهما إلى سالبة والسالبة منها إلى موجبتها إن كانت كليّة فكليّة، وإن كانت جزئية فجزئية، لكن شرط أن تحفظ الكمية والمقدم والتالي بحالهما إن كانت متصلة وإن تغيرت الكيفية وتبدل أحد الجزأين بنقيضه أيهما كان إلا أن تكون القضية متصلة موجبة والتالي فيها أعم من المقدم فإنه يجب أن يكون المأخوذ نقيضه لا نقيض المقدم، وإن شئت بيّنت ذلك في الكل بالخلف بأن تبين أنه إن لم يصدق كون المحكوم به في قوة الأصل وإلا فالصادق نقيضه وهو مما لا يصدق معه الأصل في الكل، ونحن نبين ذلك في قلب الموجبة المتصلة إلى سالبتها، وأنت تعرفه في الكل فنقول إذا قلنا إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان فهو في قوة قولنا: إن كان إنساناً فليس بحيوان وإلا فالصادق نقيضه وهو أنه إن كان إنساناً فليس بحيوان)، وقد قيل إنه إن كان إنساناً فهو (حيوان) وهو خلف
With necessity or possibility, and if the proposition is modal and the parts are absolute, then its example from the connected is our saying: by necessity or possibility 'if B is A then J is D', and from the disconnected: by necessity or possibility 'either B is A or J is D'. As for the third, because the proposition may be true while its parts are false, and may be false while its parts are true, as we explained before. And since you have known the two conditionals, their qualities, quantities, contradictories, and subcontraries as you knew in categoricals, then know that it is valid to convert the affirmative of each one to a negative, and the negative to its affirmative – if it is universal then universal, and if particular then particular – but on condition that you preserve the quantity and the antecedent and consequent in their states if it is connected, even if the quality changes, and you replace one of the two parts with its contradictory, whichever it may be, except if the proposition is a connected affirmative and the consequent is more general than the antecedent, then what is taken must be its contradictory, not the contradictory of the antecedent. And if you wish, you can demonstrate that in all by contradiction, by showing that if what is judged is not true in the force of the original, then the true is its contradictory, and that is something with which the original in all is not true. And we demonstrate that in converting a connected affirmative to its negative, and you know it for all, so we say: if we say 'if this is a human then it is an animal,' it is equivalent to our saying 'if it is a human then it is not an animal' – otherwise the true is its contradictory, namely that if it is a human then it is not an animal. And it was said that if it is a human then it is an animal, which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the conversion (qalb) of conditional propositions (qadaya shartiyya). The author first reviews the modal types (necessity and possibility) and truth conditions of conditionals, then outlines the conversion rules. Conversion here means turning an affirmative conditional into a negative one (and vice versa) while preserving the quantity (universal/particular). For connected conditionals (muttasila), when converting, one must keep the antecedent and consequent intact except that one of them is replaced by its contradictory. However, if the consequent is more general than the antecedent in an affirmative connected conditional, the contradictory to be taken is that of the more general term (the consequent), not the antecedent. The author then provides a proof by contradiction (khalaf): if the conversion were not valid, the contradictory would hold, leading to a contradiction with the original statement. The example given is 'if this is a human, then it is an animal' which supposedly converts to 'if it is a human, then it is not an animal' – this example is problematic and likely corrupted in the OCR; logically the correct conversion should be 'if it is a human, then it is not a non-animal' or similar. Nonetheless, the author proceeds to illustrate the proof by contradiction: assuming the original is true, if the converted is false, its contradictory ('if it is a human then it is not an animal') would be true, which contradicts the original. This demonstrates the validity of the conversion rule.

Key Terms
قلب (qalb): Immediate inference by converting a proposition while preserving truth, here specifically turning an affirmative conditional into a negative one or vice versa. [Note: In later texts, qalb often refers to conversion of categorical propositions (aks); here it is applied to conditionals.]
متصلة (muttasila): A connected conditional proposition of the form 'if A then B'. [Note: No significant shift in meaning across the tradition.]
منفصلة (munfasila): A disconnected conditional proposition of the form 'either A or B' (exclusive or inclusive). [Note: No significant shift.]
خلف (khalaf): Proof by contradiction, showing that denial of the conclusion leads to a contradiction with the premises. [Note: Used consistently in logic; no shift.]
نقيض (naqid): The contradictory of a term or proposition, e.g., the negation. [Note: Stable term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Replace the antecedent with its contradictory.
B) Replace the consequent with its contradictory.
C) Replace both parts with their contradictories.
D) Do not change any part, only change the quality.

Key Takeaway
Conversion of conditionals preserves quantity and truth value when one part is replaced by its contradictory, with special care when the consequent is more general.

Conversion Between Connected and Disconnected Conditionals
Author details rules for converting between muttasila and munfasila with different qualities and special case of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert between connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila) conditionals in all cases, including same quality, different quality, and the special case of possibility.

Core Idea
Conversion between conditionals requires careful attention to quality, generality, and possibility.

We have learned how to convert within the same type of conditional. Now the author extends this to converting between connected and disconnected conditionals, with detailed rules for various cases. This is a powerful tool for transforming propositions in logical arguments. We will walk through each conversion type methodically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type and quality of the given proposition
Determine if it is a connected conditional (muttasila, e.g., 'if A then B') or a disconnected conditional (munfasila, e.g., 'either A or B'). Also note if it is affirmative or negative.
Quality may be same or different between the source and target.
2
Apply same-quality conversion rules
For converting between conditionals of the same quality (affirmative to affirmative or negative to negative), delete the connector and insert the disconnector (or vice versa), and replace one part with its contradictory. If the conditional is connected and affirmative with a more general consequent, take the contradictory of the more general (the consequent).
This rule applies to both two-part and multi-part disjunctives.
3
Handle multiple parts in disconnected to connected conversion
When converting a disconnected with more than two parts, you have two options: (1) place the contradictory of one part as antecedent and the rest as a disconnected consequent, or (2) place the original part as antecedent and the rest as a conjunction of categoricals or a disconnected. Swapping antecedent and consequent is also allowed.
The method ensures logical equivalence in most cases.
4
Apply different-quality conversion rules
When converting between different qualities (affirmative to negative or vice versa), follow the same steps as same-quality but also change the quality of the resulting proposition. For connected to disconnected, delete the connector, insert the disconnector, and change the quality. For disconnected to connected, with two parts treat as connected, with more than two, adjust accordingly.
The quality change is indicated by 'ghayyarat al-kayfiyya'.
5
Recognize the exception in possibility matter
Converting a negative connected conditional to an affirmative disconnected conditional is not always valid in matter of possibility (maddat al-imkan). For example, 'possibly it is not that if this is a human then it is a writer' may be true, but 'either this is a human or it is a writer' may not be true. This shows that disconnection is not simply the negation of connection.
This exception highlights the modal nature of conditionals.

وكما أنه يصح قلب الموجبة من كل واحدة منها إلى سالبتها إلى الموجبة فاعلم أنه قد يصح أيضاً قلب كل واحدة منهما إلى الأخرى، على اختلاف كيفياتها، فيصح قلب الموجبة إلى الموجبة والسالبة إلى السالبة والموجبة إلى السالبة والسالبة إلى الموجبة، وسواء كانت الأجزاء موجبات أو سوالب أو مختلطات إن اتحدت الكيفية، فإن أردت قلب الموجبة إلى الموجبة أو السالبة إلى السالبة فإما أن تريد قلب المتصلة إلى المتصلة أو قلب المنفصلة إلى المنفصلة، فإن أردت قلب المتصلة إلى المنفصلة فطريقك أن تحذف أداة الاتصال وتدخل أداة الانفصال، وتبدل أحد الجزأين بنقيضه أيهما كان إلا أن تكون القضية موجبة والتالي فيها أعمّ من المقدم، فالمأخوذ يجب أن يكون نقيض الأعم دون الأخص، وإن أردت قلب المنفصلة إلى المتصلة حذفت أداة الانفصال وأدخلت أداة الاتصال مع أخذ نقيض أحد الجزأين أيهما كان إن كانت ذات جزئين فقط، وإن كانت أكثر من ذلك فأنت مخير بين أن تضع نقيض أحد الأجزاء مقدماً أو عينه، فإن كان الأول فيكون المقدم حَمْلياً والتالي منفصلة موجبة من الباقي، وإن كان الثاني فالمقدم أيضاً حَمْلي لكن التالي قد يكون من حَمْليات إن جعلته نقيض الباقي ، وقد تكون منفصلة موجبة من نقيض الباقي مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وإن شئت أن تعكس الحال بأن تجعل ما كان مقدماً تالياً، وما كان تالياً مقدماً فلا بأس، ولا فرق في هذا بین أن يكون موجبة أو سالبة إلا بأخذ نقيض الأعم من الأجزاء السالبة المنفصلة إن كان دون الأخص منهما، وأما إن اختلفت الكيفية فإن أردت قلب الموجب إلى السالب أو السالب إلى الموجب، فإما أن تريد قلب المتصلة إلى المنفصلة أو بالعكس، فإن أردت قلب المتصلة إلى المنفصلة فإن كانت المتصلة موجبة حذفت أداة الاتصال وأدخلت أداة الانفصال وغيرت الكيفية، وإن كانت سالبة حذفت أداة الاتصال وأدخلت أداة الانفصال وغيرت الكيفية، وإن أردت قلب المنفصلة إلى متصلة فإما أن تكون ذات جزئين فقط أو أكثر، فإن كان الأول فعلت فيها كما علمناك في المتصلة، وإن كان الثاني فإما أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة وضعت عين أحد الأجزاء مقدماً والتالي منفصلة موجبة من الباقي مع تغيير الكيفية، وإن شئت أن تحول التالي والمقدم تالياً فلا حرج، وعلى كلا التقديرين فالقضية تكون من حملية ومنفصلة موجبة وإن كانت سالبة جعلت أحد أجزاء المنفصلة مقدماً في المتصلة والتالي منفصلة سالبة من الباقي، وإن شئت أن تقدم وتؤخر فلا بأس، وعلى كلا التقديرين فالقضية تكون من حملية ومنفصلة سالبة، وما أشرنا إليه من أنواع القلب فمطرد في جميع المواد ما خلا قلب السالب المتصل إلى الموجب المنفصل في مادة الإمكان، فإنه قد يصدق قولنا: (بالإمكان ليس إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو كاتب) ولا يصدق قولنا: إما أن يكون هذا إنساناً وإما أن يكون كاتباً لا بالضرورة ولا بالإمكان من جهة أن الإنسان ضروري للكاتب، فكيف يكون كاتباً ولا يكون ومن هاهنا لم يكن الانفصال هو سلب الاتصال كما ظن، وإن كان قد يلزم إنساناً،
And just as it is valid to convert the affirmative of each one to its negative and the negative to its affirmative, so know that it is also valid to convert each one to the other, with difference of their qualities. So it is valid to convert affirmative to affirmative, negative to negative, affirmative to negative, and negative to affirmative, whether the parts are affirmative or negative or mixed if the quality is the same. If you wish to convert affirmative to affirmative or negative to negative, then either you want to convert connected to connected or disconnected to disconnected. If you want to convert connected to disconnected, your method is to delete the connector and insert the disconnector, and replace one of the two parts with its contradictory, whichever it may be, except if the proposition is affirmative and the consequent is more general than the antecedent, then what is taken must be the contradictory of the more general, not the less general. If you want to convert disconnected to connected, you delete the disconnector and insert the connector while taking the contradictory of one of the two parts, whichever it may be, if it has only two parts. If it has more than that, you are free between placing the contradictory of one part as antecedent or its very self. If the former, then the antecedent is categorical and the consequent is an affirmative disconnected from the remainder; if the latter, then the antecedent is also categorical but the consequent may be from categoricals if you make it the contradictory of the remainder, or it may be an affirmative disconnected from the contradictory of the remainder, preventing emptiness without conjunction. And if you wish to reverse the situation by making what was antecedent a consequent and what was consequent an antecedent, that is fine. And there is no difference in this between affirmative and negative except by taking the contradictory of the more general from the parts of the negative disconnected if it is more general than the less general of them. And if the quality differs, then if you wish to convert affirmative to negative or negative to affirmative, then either you want to convert connected to disconnected or vice versa. If you want to convert connected to disconnected, then if the connected is affirmative, delete the connector and insert the disconnector and change the quality; if it is negative, delete the connector and insert the disconnector and change the quality. If you want to convert disconnected to connected, then either it has only two parts or more. If the former, you do as we taught you for the connected. If the latter, then either the disconnected is affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, you place the very self of one of the parts as antecedent and the consequent is an affirmative disconnected from the remainder with change of quality; if you wish to make the consequent and antecedent as consequent, no harm. In both cases, the proposition consists of a categorical and an affirmative disconnected. If it is negative, you make one of the parts of the disconnected as antecedent in the connected and the consequent is a negative disconnected from the remainder; if you wish to advance and delay, no harm. In both cases, the proposition consists of a categorical and a negative disconnected. And what we have indicated of the types of conversion is applicable in all matters except the conversion of the negative connected to the affirmative disconnected in the matter of possibility. For it may be true our saying 'possibly it is not that if this is a human then it is a writer' but not true our saying 'either this is a human or it is a writer', neither by necessity nor by possibility, because a human is necessary for a writer, so how can it be a writer and not be? And from this, the disconnection is not the negation of connection as supposed, although it may necessitate a human.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on converting between connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila) conditional propositions, covering various cases of quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (number of parts). The author first reiterates the permissibility of converting within the same type, then moves to cross-type conversion. For converting a connected to a disconnected with the same quality, one deletes the connector (in) and inserts the disconnector (imma), replacing one part with its contradictory. The exception is when the consequent is more general than the antecedent; then the contradictory of the more general is taken. For converting a disconnected to a connected, with two parts, one takes the contradictory of one part. With more than two parts, one has options: either place the contradictory of one part as antecedent and the rest as a disconnected consequent, or place the original part as antecedent and the rest as a conjunction of categoricals or a disconnected. The author also allows swapping antecedent and consequent. When the quality differs (affirmative to negative or vice versa), the method is similar but requires changing the quality. The passage concludes with a notable exception: converting a negative connected (e.g., 'possibly not if human then writer') to an affirmative disconnected (e.g., 'either human or writer') is not always valid in matter of possibility, because the original may be true while the conversion false. This shows that disconnection is not merely the negation of connection, as some think. The last line is cut off but likely continues the discussion.

Conversion of Conditionals and Reductio ad Absurdum
Author explains conversion of conditionals and reductio method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a conjunctive conditional into its disjunctive form and use reductio ad absurdum to verify its truth.

Core Idea
Conditionals can be tested by converting them to disjunctions and reducing to absurdity.

In this passage, the author explains a powerful technique: converting conjunctive conditionals into disjunctive forms and then using reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) to check their validity. Understanding this method sharpens your ability to evaluate logical arguments in Islamic sciences, from juristic reasoning to theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional proposition
Determine whether the proposition is conjunctive (if-then) or disjunctive (either-or). For example, 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden' is a conjunctive conditional.
2
Convert conjunctive to disjunctive
According to the author, the disjunctive equivalent of 'If A then B' is 'Either A or not B'. Write out the converted form.
For 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden', the conversion is 'Either it is wine or it is not forbidden'.
3
Assume the negation
To test the original, assume its contradictory is true. For the disjunctive equivalent, find its contradictory (negation). The contradictory of 'Either A or not B' is 'It is not the case that either A or not B'.
4
Derive a contradiction
Show that the negation leads to an inconsistency. In the example, the negation resolves to 'Either A or B', which is opposed to the original conjunction.
The author states that this contradiction demonstrates the validity of the original conditional.
5
Apply reductio ad absurdum
Conclude that the original conditional is true because its negation leads to a contradiction. This is the method of khalaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني: في القضايا الشرطية من سلب الاتصال، وإن شئت أن تبين ذلك كله بالخلف قلت إن لم يصدق أن القضية المفروضة في قوة الأصل المفروض وإلا فالصادق نقيضها، ثم تنظر إلى ما في قوة ذلك النقيض من السوالب إن كان موجباً أو الموجبات إن كان سالباً كما عرفناك، فإنك تجده مكذباً للأصل وبه تبين الخلف، ونحن نبين ذلك أيضاً قلب الموجبة المتصلة إلى الموجبة المنفصلة، وأنت تعرفه في الكل، فنقول (إذا قلنا إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان) إن لم يصدق قولنا في قوته إما أن يكون إنساناً وإما أن لا يكون حيواناً) صدق نقيضه، وهو أنه ليس إما أن يكون إنساناً وإما أن لا يكون حيواناً وأنت تنبهت لما أشرنا إليه من قلب المتصلات بعضها إلى بعض، وكذا في المنفصلات علمت أن هذا النقيض في قوة قولنا: (إما أن يكون إنساناً وإما أن يكون حيواناً)، وهذا العناد مناقض للاتصال خلف
The second art: On conditional propositions. Regarding the negation of connection, and if you wish to demonstrate all that by reduction to absurdity, you say: if it is not true that the supposed proposition is equivalent to the supposed original, then the true one is its contradictory. Then you look at what is equivalent to that contradictory from the negatives if it is affirmative, or the affirmatives if it is negative, as we taught you. You will find it falsifying the original, and by that the reduction is shown. And we also demonstrate that by converting the affirmative conjunctive to the affirmative disjunctive, and you know it in all. So we say: If we say 'If this is a human, then it is an animal', if our statement 'In its force, it is either a human or not an animal' is not true, then its contradictory is true, which is 'It is not that it is either a human or not an animal'. And you have become aware of what we indicated regarding conversion of conjunctives into each other. Similarly, in disjunctives, you know that this contradictory is equivalent to our statement 'Either it is a human or it is an animal'. And this contradiction is opposed to conjunction, as a reduction.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage explains the method of converting conditional propositions (qadaya shartiyya) and using reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) to test their logical strength. The author first defines the process: to demonstrate the validity of a conditional, one can assume its negation and derive a contradiction. He then provides an example: the conjunctive conditional 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' is converted into a disjunctive 'Either it is a human or it is not an animal' (the disjunctive equivalent). If the original is false, its contradictory is true. The contradictory of the disjunctive equivalent is 'It is not the case that either it is a human or it is not an animal', which resolves to 'Either it is a human or it is an animal' (by De Morgan-like transformation). This contradiction shows the original conditional holds. The author emphasizes that this technique applies to all conditionals, both conjunctive and disjunctive. An Islamic example: 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden' can be tested by converting to 'Either it is wine or it is not forbidden' and reducing to absurdity.

خلف
شرطية متصلة
شرطية منفصلة

Key Takeaway
Conversion of conditionals to disjunctions and reductio ad absurdum provide a systematic way to verify logical relationships.

Contradiction and Conversion of Conditionals
Author defines contradiction and two types of conversion for conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform simple conversion and contraposition on a conditional proposition from Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Conversion of conditionals is not a free swap; it follows rules analogous to categoricals.

Just as we learned to convert categorical propositions, conditional propositions also have conversion operations. In this lesson, based on the author's concise statement, we will unpack the two types of conversion: simple conversion (aks al-istiwa) and contraposition (aks al-naqid). Understanding these helps in analyzing conditional statements in Islamic legal reasoning and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall contradiction rules from categoricals
The author states that contradiction of conditionals is the same as in categoricals. In categoricals, contradiction requires opposite quality and quantity. For conditionals, it means swapping the truth value: the contradictory of 'If P then Q' is 'P and not Q' (not a conditional). Keeping this in mind helps clarify conversion.
This is not a conversion step but a reminder that contradiction rules carry over.
2
Identify simple conversion (aks al-istiwa)
Simple conversion swaps the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī) while keeping the quality (affirmative/negative) and truth value unchanged. For a conditional 'If P then Q', simple conversion would yield 'If Q then P'. But note: this is not always logically valid; the author likely implies it holds for certain types like biconditionals or when the relation is symmetric.
In Islamic logic, this operation is defined but its validity depends on the connective.
3
Identify contraposition (aks al-naqid)
Contraposition makes the contradictory (negation) of the antecedent the consequent, and the contradictory of the consequent the antecedent. So from 'If P then Q', we get 'If not Q then not P'. This is always valid for any conditional.
This is the same as modus tollens in syllogistic form.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the conditional statement: 'If the water is pure (P), then it is permissible for wudu (Q)'. The simple conversion 'If it is permissible for wudu, then it is pure' is not generally true (e.g., pure sand can be used). But the contrapositive 'If it is not permissible for wudu, then it is not pure' is valid.
Always test with real examples to see the logical relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تناقض الشرطيات فعلى ما عرف في الحمليات، وكذا أيضاً تعاكسها - أعني عكس الاستواء - وهو تبديل المقدم والتالي بالمقدم مع بقاء الكيفية والصدق والكذب بحاله، وعكس النقيض وهو جعل نقيض المقدم تالياً ونقيض التالي مقدماً، واعتبر ذلك بنفسك.
As for the contradiction of conditionals, it is as known in categoricals. And likewise their conversion – I mean simple conversion – which is exchanging the antecedent and the consequent while preserving the quality and truth value. And contraposition is making the contradictory of the antecedent the consequent and the contradictory of the consequent the antecedent. And consider that yourself.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage addresses two operations on conditional propositions: contradiction (tanāquḍ) and conversion (ta‘ākus). The author first states that the rules for contradicting conditionals are the same as those for categoricals (i.e., contradicting by changing quality and quantity). Then he defines two types of conversion. The first is 'aks al-istiwa' (simple conversion), where the antecedent and consequent are swapped without altering the quality (affirmative/negative) or truth value. The second is 'aks al-naqid (contraposition), where the contradictory (negation) of the antecedent becomes the consequent, and the contradictory of the consequent becomes the antecedent. The author leaves it to the reader to apply these. For an Islamic example: the conditional 'If it is water, then it is pure' can be simply converted to 'If it is pure, then it is water'? No, that does not preserve truth (it may be pure but not water). Simple conversion of conditionals is not generally valid—the author likely means conversion of the disjunctive form? However, the text states it preserves quality and truth value, so perhaps it applies only to specific types. Alternatively, the example could be: 'If it is not a camel, then it is a human' (false) converting to 'If it is a human, then it is not a camel' (maybe true). Contraposition: 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden' becomes 'If it is not forbidden, then it is not wine'.
